RATING: 7
So, I am rating this game after one play. 

The theme is so thick, and the gameplay is superb. But boy, is the game rough. We are not used to that amount of player interaction, especially not of the backstabbing kind.

I had a weird feeling after completing the first game. Joy and sorrow, but such is the game of thrones.

I really want to give the game an 8, and I hope the next game will upgrade the score, but for now 7 is the feeling the one game left me with.

My wife would probably give it a higher score, she is a great AGOT geek, and she actually won our first game.

RATING: 6
Update after 2 Plays: I think I'm done with this game.  Dropped from an 8 to a 6.  This game is just too long.  First game was 6P, quit at 3 hours after round 7.  Second game was 6P, quit at 4 hours after round 6.  It's also fun for the first several rounds as you build up, form alliances, and make your first attacks.  Then it just starts to feel like your taking and re-taking the same areas, trading back and forth, over and over.  No sense of progress.  And the ending feels completely arbitrary.  Someone would happen to win at the end of any round but if you go another round or another, it would probably always shift.  Not planning to play this again.

Possibly should rate this a 4 but I might give it one more go, perhaps with less players.

RATING: 8
This game is way more complex than it should have been.  I imagine there are many non-gamer GoT fans out there who bought this, only to have a horrible experience trying to learn the rules with their friends.

With that said, it's a very good game.  I think the combat system is the best of any game I've played: quick, simple, deterministic and with just a bit of tension from not knowing which card your opponent will select.

I've played once at 3 players and once at 6.  6 was good, but you only really interact with the players next to you.  4 or 5 might be the sweet spot.

The game can go fairly long.

RATING: 10
My favorite board game for 3-6 players. Takes the impeccable mechanics of Diplomacy and streamlines them to create a game that can be completed in 4 hours (rather than 14). Also incorporates tight, game-theory-grounded, secret bidding and hand-management mechanics and brings in the color and flavor of Westeros.

RATING: 9
A streamlined version of Diplomacy, with various limitations ensuring a more balanced play, with less lagging behind of weaker players.
The theme is very strong, and is highly suggested for players who love A Song of Ice and Fire.
Relatively long play time, and requires 5-6 players to work well.
Will not work well with honest players, or players that do not like direct confrontations (war in multi player games).

RATING: 6
Superbly thematic and can be a lot of fun to play but has a laundry list of flaws that mean it's unlikely to hit the table again any time soon. Too long, players can be knocked out early and cannot recover, some overly complex rules, balance issues at some player counts (4/5). Suffers from the same issue as most 'diplomatic' area control games - games boil down to a dog pile of mutual destruction and whoever isn't kept in check will likely win.

This game can cause some hurt feelings when long-time alliances are broken with a surprise attack, so I wouldn't recommend this game for everyone.

RATING: 5
I played this game just three times, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Good thematic wargame, that will appeal a lot to all the fans of the books/tv serie. Being not a fan of "A Game of Thrones" myself, the theme is nothing that excites me particularly, but I see and appreciate the effort, that the designer put to integrate the theme in the mechanisms.

As far as the machanisms are concerned, it's a well designed game, but nothing that I'll go out of my way to play. The first reason for this is the lenght: the game is too long for what it offers me. I like the card combat a lot, but I'm not a fan as much of the orders or even more of the event cards and the supply mechanism. I don't like the way the different kinds of unit work and I also don't like the fact that you're not involved in the game with all the players. You actually interact only with the players that are close to you on the board, while there's very few or nothing you can do about those that are "geographically" far from you... and while this is fine in a game like 7 Wonders, I'm not sure it is in a war game. These things simply do not click for me.

Of many war games A Game of Thrones is not the one I'd like to play if I could choose. I like the components a lot, some ideas are neat, but all together comes out strangely mediocre. I'm sure that this game really shines if you enjoy the theme, but - if you don't - there's many other games you can choose from, that are actually mechanically better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is an area control game based on the Game of Thrones universe. Don't be fooled, this game is not an adventure game, so do not expect to give back the story from the books or TV-series. Basically you will start with one of six noble houses of Westeros, then through careful planning will assign actions to your armies on map. Then after the planning is done, in turn everyone has the chance to execute one action having in mind that one must control seven cities or strongholds to instantly win the game, else it ends after ten rounds. The downside of the game is the long downtime of the planning phase, and it doesn't really work well with fewer then six players. The game can take easily six hours to play out, but if you are a fan of intrigue and warfare it is a perfect game.

RATING: 6
I'll start by saying, I don't like the IP at all. The actual storyline frustrates me. However, this game did a good job of putting the player into the struggle, jockeying for position on the throne, fighting military battles, and using covert networks for information.

The game is well made, and a good game for 6 players. The length is long (which I knew going into the game) and there's a bit of a kingmaker aspect to it, but all in all, the game is well made, with good bits and I'm happy to play it a couple times a year.

RATING: 7
The game looks good - it's a bit busy in places and is certainly an FFG game with all the chits and bits. 

Mechanically, its interesting - I take if you played it a lot, or very deliberately, there are best plays early, but I've specifically refrained from knowing a lot about the game so I can enjoy it two or three times a year. 

The 'intrigue' angle is fairly weak to be honest. I think the game is all about hedging and timing your run, which some might say A Song of Ice and Fire is all about, but I think a lot of people expect backstab-counterbackstab, and you just can't recover easily.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
JEU DE GUERRE - Jusqu'à 6 Joueurs

Depuis vos territoires, vous attribuer des actions a vos troupes (un choix de 5 actions...raid; marche (ou attaque); défense; soutenir; couronne (ou taxes) et maximum 3 fois chaque). Il se peut que vous n'ayez pas assez d'actions possible si votre territoire est trop grand. Le jeu dure 10 tours maximum ou 7 châteaux sous sa gouverne.

Lors d'un combat, les troupes choisissent un héros et en additionnant le total des points de troupes,le gagnant repousse les troupe de l'autre dans une contrée adjacente appartenant au perdant. Le nombre de troupes est limité ainsi que la taille maximale de vos armées sont règlementés. Le jeu nécessite des Alliances et des traitrises

Le temps de 180 minutes sur la boite est grandement sous-estimé. Compter environ 1 heure par joueur... cela pourrais être plus, une partie peut durer facilement de 6 a 8 heures si personne ne se fait éliminer rapidement. Prenez donc votre journée complète.

Les familles qui sont dans le milieu du plateau sont désavantagée géographiquement et devront utiliser des alliances.  

Le pouvoir de l'épée débalance le jeu si les enchères ne sortent pas souvent.

RATING: 10

Once you're rocking these variants it's a really great game. Also, Fantasy Flight Games is a great company with superb customer service.

RATING: 10
If you can get 6 people together for 4+ hours, there is nothing short of an orgy that would be more satisfying to do than play thrones.  The main mechanic is stolen from Diplomacy, but the theme and flavor of the additional mechanics make it 100x more fun to play than Kissinger's favorite pastime.  In addition to the war game, you've got bidding, hand management, asymmetrical board positions, strong social play, and a light co-op element.  There's an expansion that makes it playable with 4 players, but you really want 5-6.

RATING: 5
This was a strange one. The person who brought it didn't quite know the rules, having only played it once several months before, and then not read the rules when she bought it herself. So it's possible that this colors my perception of the game.

But in play, the game felt like the designers kept hitting balance issues when they were playtesting and, to solve that, instituted a wide variety of bizarre, non-thematic choices. I'd say that it felt like an abstract strategy, due to how divorced the rules were from reality, except those generally have clean, simple rules. This had rules coming out of its ears.

Apparently, it was designed as a variant of Diplomacy. But instead of requiring that multiple people attack, in order to win a battle, the designers chose to make battle more like Sumo wrestling, and you just push people back if you attack them with too few troops. This has the side-effect of giving the person you attacked a free movement, to expand onto more territory (which is exactly not the outcome you would expect of winning a battle - even if it wasn't a complete massacre). And in order to prevent you from bringing a lot of troops, they added a rule which basically prevents you from putting your troops together (you can feed an infinite number of troops, unless you put three of them on the same square, and suddenly that breaks the bank).

The end result is, as it is in Diplomacy, you really need to have allies attack with you if you want to win a fight. But that occurs as a side-effect of a byzantine set of rules, rather than simply being made into a straight-forward requirement. It makes it very non-intuitive to play and hard to teach, and more liable for players to end up playing wrong until they've played the games a few times.

Overall, I didn't love it. I feel like you'd do better to toss the combat rules which come in the box, ignore the feeding the troops rule, and play with regular Diplomacy rules for combat. Maybe the rest of the game is fine. I don't remember anything much more about it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
I am a big fan of the Game of Thrones/A Song of Ice and Fire series and while I do enjoy the board game overall I wish it did some things better.

For example, I feel that alliances are broken or betrayed as an "inevitability" and not a "possibility" since there is often a last-minute dash for victory. I would prefer it if there was more emphasis on the benefits of an alliance rather than it being just a temporary reprieve before you inevitably get screwed over. 

It would also be nice if they had introduced wedding arrangements or a similar betrothal element since that forms a large part of the reason why certain families are allied to others etc.

In addition, there is some degree imbalance between the different houses which is made pretty dull by the lack of different house abilities. So what tends to happen is people say "Everyone hates the Lannisters in the book - so let's hate the Lannisters in the game" and you're done for.

Battles are more often than not purely decided by a card which kind of sucks.

Suffers from a real "kingmaker" problem and also player elimination. What's worse though is when you are defeated but not eliminated and so have to sit through countless turns until the game is over. Not good at all.

The rule book could have been much clearer with larger text and in general the game lasts too long for what it is. 

You can also screw yourself over by sheer bad luck. For example: I make moves to conquer areas with barrels for Supply but then the Muster card comes up first. Despite the fact that I am rolling in Supply barrels I am shackled to the same low army limit purely because Muster came up before Supply. Also, you can bid like crazy to get ahead following a Clash of Kings but then the same thing can happen on the very next turn meaning you lose everything.

Player interaction is relatively poor. You engage closely with one or two adjacent players but if you're Stark in the North you are unlikely to ever come into contact with Martell in the South.

Finally, if you are losing by the end of the game (largely due to the target on your back as the leader) there is practically no chance to catch up again. So in the end you just have to sit it out as you slowly wait for the end. Monopoly has the same problem and that's not much fun either.

I think "Rex: Final Days of an Empire" scratches a similar itch and does it better, but admittedly the theme of AGoT is probably more accessible to people.

RATING: 8
Amazing game. Feels a lot like the stories with the amount of alliances being forged and broken turn by turn. Overall it does an excellent job of blending and balancing a number of common mechanics found in many other war games similar to this (the unit production, army formation, and general negotiation that goes on through them). The Westeros cards do a great job of making rounds not feel too static from one another creating spontaneity that each player should prepare for. The house cards are equally interesting to manage as you struggle to decide which leaders to send into battle.

Overall a lot of fun and this edition comes packed with a ton of great components that help to streamline play.

RATING: 3
Diplomacy Ameritrashed. On the one hand, you don't exactly need seven players, and it doesn't have to take an entire afternoon to play (in person).

It does add a lot of new fiddly stuff on the basic Diplomacy model, for example bidding for special abilities, cards often randomly disallow certain orders, etc. I guess I would appreciate it if I were a fan of the series, but I'm not, and it just feels like cruft on the elegant engine of Diplomacy.

More problematic is that it's unbalanced at lower player counts. So the game I played had the white guys, the yellow guys and the red guys (again, not a GoT fan). I was the red guys, and I got squeezed out. White took their stuff and didn't much after that, while yellow was able to take the sea square that let them take four castles (out of seven) for the win. Yellow, who owned the game and said that this typical for three and four player games.

Having said that, it was enjoyable enough that I certainly would like to play again, if only at higher player counts.

RATING: 9.5
Preliminary thoughts:
+ Conflict, alliances, treachery, but always with a single victor (prevents the tendency for couples/friends to simply team up because they like each other). 
+ Very innovative and elegant (though complicated) implementation of defeat (retreats/routs/'death'), 'supply and demand' (in this case, supply in the sense of the fixed amount of supply, power, and mustering/victory point icons, and demand in the sense of competition for available resources rather than an actual market mechanism), 'money' (power) and bidding, and even a cooperative aspect (wildlings). 
+ Very thematic, and many of the complex rules actually make sense given what they are meant to represent.
+ Feast for Crows expansion adds some flexibility to the game (different feel, better support for four players).
- Long play time.
- More complex than some might like.
- Confrontational / cut-throat.
[3-6p. 6p*. 180m.]
(2014-03)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a deep strategic war game but requires patience to set up and to teach the rules. When everyone is comfortable with the rules, Game of Thrones has a great rhythm. I love games with everyone's undivided attention, and people are very focused knowing that they either win the Game of Thrones or die.

The map only balances well with a high player count (see the forum for ideas to balance game play with fewer players). 

I don't know how much this relates to my inexperience, but the influence tracks seemed imbalanced, harming the bidding experience. In one game, I was too low on the King's Court and there wasn't an auction for a long time, leaving me feeling highly dependent on the mercy of others because I was unable to muster and mobilize units. 

If you are introducing the game to new players, I have two recommendations. One is to deflect all questions about the tracks until everyone is comfortable with the core mechanics, as they make a game that really isn't too complicated seem overly complex. My other recommendation is that you download a house cards cheat sheet from the files section and give a copy to everyone.

RATING: 8
Esta segunda edición del juego de Tronos es una amalgama muy bien trenzada  de diversos elementos del juego base y las expansiones. Es la guinda donde se cristaliza el objetivo de hacer el juego más dinámico y fluido. Y lo ha logrado. No cometer crasos errores sigue siendo determinista para alzarse con la victoria. Se mueve por el filo de la navaja en el equilibrio entre el control y el caos. 

El juego está brillantemente decorado y esto hace que respiremos el aroma literario en que está basado por todos los lados. Felicitaciones a Fantasy Flight. Han unido una gran recopilación aquí con todo lo que tenían. Es un juego que difícilmente una ampliación puede aportarle algo excepto complejidad y duración.

Solo lo he jugado a seis donde el equilibrio de poderes es patente. El enigma de la cantidad de jugadores puede ser el problema técnico importante que aún queda por pulir en el juego.

RATING: 8
Almost as good as Diplomacy!

EDIT: This is my group's favorite game to play, and I've reduced my rating slightly after many plays. It is like Diplomacy, but a Diplomacy-lite that waters down the game too much to be perfect. 

EDIT: Unlike Diplomacy, where game balance between Great Powers can be entirely mitigated by player skill and negotiation, Game of Thrones has a terrible balance issue with the Lannisters, Greyjoys, and Starks. The Greyjoys wield the executioner's axe over the Lannisters, and while the Starks are a counterweight to the Greyjoys, there is very little incentive for the Starks and Greyjoys to fight, and I've almost never seen it happen. 

The one good thing about the game is that it's 5 player balance is very good if you have one less player than required.

RATING: 8.5
A Game of Thrones simply drips with theme: and this is the stuff of any good game. 

It’s so deeply inspired and finely tuned that, while playing, you can almost breathe the same atmosphere and get the same feeling as while reading Martin’s Song of Ice and Fire: during the game, Westeros and both its most loved and hated characters become nearly alive.

By including both expansions to the basic game, this second edition not only improves a little on the previous one but also gets much more enjoyable overall, even though it still suffers from that «Risiko syndrome» that makes it hard for a player who fell behind to catch up with the leader.

Play with a group of friends who read all the (published) books and be prepared to a very long session to fully enjoy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
At the beginning you will love the game, especially if you like the Game of Thrones series. The design is very beautiful and the mechanics are interesting. Then by every game you will realize the disadvantages and the real problems of the gameplay - the worst thing is that the game works the same way every time and doesn't bring anything new. Usually patt situations result, where you can't really do anything, or by doing the same actions you come back to the same results. Mechanics should be changed radically in order to bring some variety in the game. It's interesting that from the beginning the game was far behind in the rating around 300 position, then I guess the fans of GoT series came :) Unfortunately there are much much better games out there, this one doesn't worth more than ~5 games playing. It's a pity.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Risk for grown-ups as I like to refer to it.  It really does make Risk look like child-play.

You are far more limited in how many armies you can have, not just in one space (which I am okay with), but overall on the board in general (one of the parts I don't really like) and there are 'orders' you give your armies that inject a large amount of strategy into the game by impacting each other in cases conflict and helping you generate more funds.  You play a general in each combat that will either modify the combat itself, or possibly hurt your enemy or help you in other ways.

Territories provide various 'resources' you'll need to win.

You bid on 'influence' tracks sometimes (determined by event cards) which will also potentially change your strategies.

Lastly there are random event cards drawn each round that can help or hinder one or more players.

You win the game by either obtaining a certain number of strongholds or whoever has the most points by the end of ten rounds.  

The only major "Con" to this game is some of the scenarios with less players... but do some looking around on the internet for some alternate setups and you'll find solutions to what you might consider problems with them.

RATING: 8.5
This is the ultimate Game of Thrones experience. Every move is vitally important. This is sort of an offspring of Diplomacy, though simplifies some of the complex mechanics of figuring out what happens during a turn. The fact that there is no luck/dice involved is a highlight for my group. Alliances, though not necessary, are usually vital throughout the game. Play doesn't necessitate knowledge of the books or tv show, but it definitely is a bonus. Only knock is that its a long game and one bad move (or broken alliance) can put someone behind early and they'll have a hard time recovering. This has made some people in my gaming group sour toward trying further plays. Playing with less than 5 really gives the advantage to the southern houses unless Greyjoy/Lannister really pressure Baratheon early on, so definite preferred player count is 6.

RATING: 7
I cannot say that the theme is great because it doesn't translate to the TV Series/books.

The Westeros map is there, the families are there, even the characters, but when you play it seems just another area control game, played by the yellow, red, blue (etc) team.

But the game itself is damn good! Tense, with lot of player confrontation. Prone to alliances that are broken as easily as they were formed. So, kind of a backstabbing game. Each faction has their character cards and that adds to the strategy of the game. 

The components are amazing, the artwork it's very good as well.

Once you play the first turn, you'll find it easier than it looks!

Since all players execute their actions simultaneously, this the the kind of game that gets better with an higher amount of players (6).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The game is mechanically well done. It offers enough strategic options, while still being somewhat streamlined. It also managed to feel thematic and being not gamey.

The player interaction is greatly amplified and guided by the design. You need to switch alliances regularly and want to stop a player falling too far behind, because that usually means someone gets too powerful.
The battles are well done and not too punishing, since you can usually just retreat. There's an interesting guessing and bluffing aspect to battles.
The planning phase also works very well and allows for some exciting bluffs and surprise moves.

I love it, but often 1 or 2 players are out of the title race early, which is clearly a problem. Also the game works best with 6 and takes a while.

Downsides are an imbalanced setup - especially the extreme start for Greyjoy/Lannister - and random supply change and recruit. It's sligthly too random. Stark can be left at 1 supply for a very long time and in general the game accelerates when supply is drawn. If no supply is drawn the game becomes too static.

The same is true for succession. Wether succession is drawn thrice in a row or even never till round 8 will shape the game greatly and not in a good way. Especially if succession is drawn twice/thrice in a row the game becomes too random.

6pl 4pl (exp)

RATING: 9
Risk on steroids (but good Christian Petersen type steroids). I was a bit uncertain about the first edition and am glad I waited for this. While there may still be expansions, perhaps they won't seem as much like patches.

First play (five player): Game is excellent, as hoped/expected - five players seemed reasonably balanced with lots of interesting decisions along with the usual wheeling and dealing. Great theme, mechanics, bits, the lot! Well done...

Incidentally, I've got the same "miss-cut" board sections that people were reporting as board "warping." FFG acknowledged the problem and my new board came and it is fine - didn't have to wait unduly long - kudos to FFG customer support.

Subsequent plays have borne out the high opinion I have of this game. Like Rex(Dune) this game needs at least five or six players to make for a good experience - ideally six. Good again on FFG for putting out a better four-player version.

RATING: 9.75
I came into this game with very high expectations...which to be honest had me a little worried. Would it be able to live up to what I was hoping for?

The answer is unequivocally yes!

Almost everything about this game is awesome. The art and map really draw you into the world of Westeros. Combat isn't a luckfest like Risk, but still has some guessing and double-guessing uncertainty with the house cards. We played with lots of little slips of paper for Ravens and it was just the right amount of diplomacy and negotiation that I was hoping for, not too drawn out and yet still existent.

Fantasy Flight did a fantastic job with their tutorial video. While it was still a lot for the people learning to take in, it made my job as teacher so much easier! We would pause whenever anyone had a question and I could clarify or explain in further detail.

There were a couple things that keep this from being a 10 at this point

1) There are so many little rules that hold everything together that sometimes people got themselves in situations where they thought they could do something but they couldn't. I know in some ways that's unavoidable, but in a long game like this, there was some frustration around the table. I don't see that being a problem if we played another couple times

2) Some people got themselves into bad situations at the beginning and then were stuck for the rest of the game. Now I know that's their own fault, but it sucked for those people to play for the next 3 hours feeling hopeless

All that being said, I LOVED this game and can't wait to schedule another play of it in. I suspect my rating might go all the way to a 10 after a couple more plays, but don't want to get ahead of myself :)

RATING: 9
Love the novel series, love the tv series, and I love this game.

Found this and thought it was helpful:

Five rules to remember:
1. Special Consolidate Power Order Token can be used to initiate mustering in its area.
2. Only one combat is allowed per march order.
3. The Messenger Raven token allows the holder to also look at the top card of the Wildling deck and place the card at the bottom of the deck if she chooses.
4. Ports with a friendly unit receive a power token (when the GoT Westeros card is resolved), as long as the adjacent sea is not occupied with enemy ships (i.e. blockaded).
5. When you raid a Consolidate Power order not only do you receive a power token, but the player losing the Consolidate Power order has to give up one of their power tokens.

Port Rules:
A Port is considered a sea area (bordering a Castle/Stronghold and a sea area proper) except for the following:

1 No more than three Ships allowed in a Port.

2 A Port belongs to the player owning the connected Castle/Stronghold and can never be attacked directly: Ships can never march into a non-friendly Port. When the Castle/Stronghold is conquered, the new owner can either destroy or take over the Ships in the Port (substituting one or more Ship units in the Port with units of the conqueror's colour).

3 Ships in a Port may not raid nor support land areas.

4 When there are friendly Ships in the Port and no enemy Ships in the connected sea area, the Port provides one Power Token when a "Consolidate Power" order is assigned to it, and when the "Game of Thrones" Westeros card is resolved, but not when the "Dark Wings, Dark Words" Westeros card is resolved.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.3
Typical bloated FFG release awash with various bits and bobs. The gameplay however is enjoyable and fun and plays as you would expect from a typical multi-player combat game like this. My concern with this game is due to the freedom allowed, gratuitous nature of combat and your ability to hurt an opponent, its largely beyond your control if you win. The tall poppie syndrome means that anyone in the lead or getting close to the victory conditions can easily be cut down and targeted. It basically boils down to who keeps the lowest profile, is attacked the least or can opportunistically pick up areas/castles/strongholds or who the group "lets" win. Ultimately winning or losing feels random and a glorified snakes and ladders or Risk. The game also requires strong situationally aware gamers to keep auto-balancing the game, the players and the leaders as mentioned above. 
All this would be manageable if the game did no take to long to play but the playing time for what it offers is way to long and offers no value.

RATING: 6
All in all a good game, but the setup for 3 and 4 players seems very unbalanced, heavily favoring the player in the south. Other player counts may be unbalanced too but less so. The map is interesting, but even with 6 players, it seems to force the game in a specific direction. The player in the north pretty much has to fight the player on the western island in order to get access to the rest of the map, unless he takes a sea route. The player on the western island knows that and thus those two always end up fighting each other, while the other players fight in the south independently. Maybe more games will show us different strategies, but so far it has been always like that.

Overall it's pretty much impossible to interact with all players, so if someone at the other end of the map is leading, you cannot do anything against them. The best you can do is talking other players into stopping them, if they somehow didn't notice.

I'm also a bit bothered by the king breaking all bidding ties, as ties happen frequently and are often decided almost arbitrarily (if the king doesn't care about the relative placement of two other players.) The king is a kingmaking mechanic. I guess that's intentional though to make they game even more about diplomatics and table talk.

RATING: 9
Wonderfully thought provoking, amazingly thematic game. I'd almost call this a "gateway" for war games, not because it's easy to learn (and not because it's necessarily a war game), but because it's so thematic and smart that you'll be left wanting more. In fact this was one game I couldn't stop thinking about after my first play.

The simultaneous placement of orders absolutely makes this game. Whenever I play I can't help giggling as I put down my command tokens, wondering what my opponents are putting down and feeling like something terrible is going to happen. Betrayals are inevitable, which only makes the game more thematic and more fun. The Iron Throne and Messenger Raven tokens are crucial to have, but can cripple you in other ways if you're not careful. Excellent.

RATING: 6
I finally got to play this game based on the very popular books and tv show. While the game isn’t as good as the show (yes, I know you cannot really compare those), it felt fairly thematic. It was actually a more euro-y game than I expected, which is definitely a plus. The game felt very tense and tight with 6 players (which I understand is by far the best number in this game), and while it was long, perhaps slightly too long, the pace was okay and the game didn’t bog down. It was probably slightly too long with 6, so I’m not sure how often I’m going to play it, but I’m definitely intrigued after my first play. It doesn’t hurt that I won as Stark as well! 

I’m not a fan of blind bidding, and against experienced players it’s hard to know how much to bid on the three tracks (and the track which grants you better actions is fiercely contested!). I like that the board is fairly balanced with castles/strongholds spread out as well as barrels, the latter preventing you from building a huge army, which worked very well. The simultaneous action selection was, as that mechanic usually is, tense and hard to plan for, but it worked pretty smoothly. Your plans can always be messed up by the other players, and you always have to take into consideration the position of the other players. If they are involved in a battle somewhere else, they probably will place those battle markers there, but you’re never sure and might get ambushed… That’s great. 

As Stark I was mainly in combat with two other houses and built up victory points, until I on the first turn of the last round rushed and got two castles in one move iirc. That felt pretty good, but if another player had been before me in the turnorder, he would have won. Overall the game was very tight although the Lannisters didn’t really stand a chance, which I understand is, unfortunately, the consensus here on BGG. Anyway, Game of Thrones is a great tv-show and a decent board game, although it’s slightly too long to get a great rating (but a 7 is a fairly good rating).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]Includes the print on demand expansion: A Feast for Crows.[/b]

A Game of Thrones is, along with Dominant Species and Eclipse, one of my favorite "dudes on a map" lengthier games. And after two plays one day after another, I'm incredibly encouraged to keep playing this game.

Unlike those other two titles, AGoT is actually best with six. It brings an element of diplomacy and negotiation to the table that the other games do not. Which, if you're a fan of the novels or the HBO show will strike you as wonderfully thematic. 

I've always been a little suspicious of Fantasy Flight's big sprawling games, but this is an incredibly tight design. Every rule is there for a reason, right down to the wildling attacks that everyone must confront and the bidding for turn order, military tiebreakers, and those crucial special orders. 

Love it, love it, love it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Simultaneously revealing orders and resolving the orders one at a time is a great system of play. The only downtime is waiting for others to decide and waiting for others to resolve combat. Unfortunately, the downtime from the former can be immense, as this game is highly prone to AP.

The combat system is simply stunning. Checking support allows plenty of alliances and backstabbing, and the simultaneous card reveals introduce interesting mindgames. Also, the area control aspect is quite tight, as you only get a few armies and a limited amount of turns. This makes each decision important.

AGoT:TBG is best played with 4 or more. 3 is ok, but there are too few interesting turns and interactions.

This game provides satisfaction about on par with Risk Legacy, but at a significantly longer game length, and for the length of gameplay, it doesn't quite provide the depth and satisfaction that War of the Ring provides. So while I really enjoy this game, it has nearly been replaced by these games in my mind. Still, those who love the franchise will like this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is basically the old game Diplomacy updated and stylized from the George R.R. Martin book series of the same name.  It adds features like fending off wildlings and a bidding system, as well as measures to keep the duration down to 2-3 hours but at it's heart it is still Diplomacy.  

As with Diplomacy, you should be careful with who you play this game with.  It's a game of bluffing and deception and some people have a hard time dealing with that.

RATING: 3
A very enjoyable game that I might have enjoyed more if I wasn't sleep-deprived. Rating may (and probably will) go up after more plays.

I played it again after a year or so with six players instead of three and it was a dreadful experience. The downtime is utterly painful, and the idea that you can't choose when you reap the rewards of the barrels you spent so much time working towards is one of the worst offenses I've ever seen in game design. I will never judge a game again when under the effects of sleep-dep, and will probably never play this again.

RATING: 7
[b][i]Finally[/i][/b] played my copy. My 2nd copy. My first copy I sold in shrink. And I'm talking about 2nd copy of 2nd edition. First edition I sold with first two expansions. I keep selling it, but then thinking about it and rebuying it. I only played first edition + 1st expansion a couple times, and 2nd expansion once. I forget the details. I definitely like having a more complete base game in revised edition. 

This is a really hard game to rate. I have fond memories of the original edition, even though I didn't play much. The game goes reeeaaaally slowly with players who aren't familiar with it, and it's hard for me to get the game to the table enough, and with the same players, to get a group of primed players. You really need to know all the combat cards of each house. Otherwise you need to look at each others' cards all the time, or else just take the chaos of not knowing what they have. That's what we did tonight, and it still took us 3 hours to make it through half the game before we called it, as some players were running out of stamina. 

Anyway, this fired Diplomacy for me. I like the planning aspect, without having to be hyper literal in what every unit does. I love things like the Influence tracks and the supply system. I like how everyone plans at the same time. That seems like it should keep things moving, but in reality there's still a fair bit of thinking when resolving actions, and especially choosing a combat card.

Basically, I'd rate this game higher if I could somehow get it played more often and get the play time down. They're interrelated, of course. But I think I'll always have a soft spot for this game, even though it could be another decade before I play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very interesting, strategic and exciting game to play, and one of the few that my wife will always say yes to playing, but also one that she often regrets doing so.

Pros:
Really captures the feel of game of thrones with intrigue, alliances and betrayals
Nice quality pieces, and nice rules for bidding, supporting, attacking.
The combat cards add cool flavor, and adds a nice bluffing element

Cons:
As many others mentioned, very long. Too long. It is a shame, as it feels like you are building towards something great, and would like more turns, but everything takes time, the calculations, decision paralysis, bidding, etc.
Some fiddly rules with ports, multiple moves, the whole Westeros deck (probably should have found a cleaner way of doing this)
Supply reconciliation is annoying and unwieldy, and keeping track of how many armies you have is the same.

Still a top strategy game, and one I love to play with the family!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
The limited amount of different actions (attack, support, defend) that you can pick each round help limit a run away winner.  I think it would play better with the optional deck that add a random modifier to combat.  Without that you look at there cards and they look at yours and you figure out what number card you each have left.  It makes combat a little too deterministic and less climatic.  The cards are a nice way of doing combat (like Kemet) but adding in some element of chance would make it more fun.

RATING: 8
After 5 plays in the last two weeks i feel that i can finally rate this game. I love the clever mechanics if the hidden betting and the hidden orders. My gamegroup is really fun to play with as alliances is made and broken constantly during the game and everyone sits deeply burried in though with the occasional paranoid stare at their opponent. There is some downsides though. My main issue is the balance. The games ive played are four player games and the balance is somewhat of. We have houseruled some of the inbalance but it still isnt quite there. The main problem being Greyjoys position. He must accept that he has to leave Stark and lannister alone so they can deal with baratheon and then sit and wait for most of the game before trying anything. Maybe this will  change as we get more experienced as has some of the other things we found unbalanced. This is my main concern and a pretty big one. Im looking forward to playing a full game. The game also has a tendency to drag a bit, although this is not a big concern for me it can be with a few players prone to AP. All in all, a really good game with fun mechanics and a lot of diplomacy and interaction.

RATING: 5
I've played this game and I didn't like it because of 2 things

1.The Westeros card have too much of an effect in the game.You might have developed a good strategy to create big armies,but there is no muster card around so all your plans go to waste.

2.The informal alliances.I like diplomacy,table talk and alliances,but it should have been implemented in the game mechanics.Having rules upon entering an alliance or exiting and the positive and negative effects should be represented with ingame mechanics.The pseudo-alliance thing makes the game feel clunky and not tight at all.

In general the game felt more of like:"Hey,we have the GoT theme.Let's figure out some mechanics so we can get it going" instead of:"Hey,we have some good mechanics here,we could try implementing the GoT theme on top of it"

The unit pieces look ugly as hell also,but this was the least of my complaints with the game

RATING: 6.5
Storage: http://shop.hobbylobby.com/products/plastic-box-with-6-compartments-406157/

Arryn overlay for expansion. https://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/151193/arryn-overlay

# of players and breakdown on experience/funness: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/908561/number-players-breakdown

Rating as of Jan '15 - 6.5. Owned since June '14 and only played 3 times at the time of this rating. I like this game a lot better with the Feast for Crows expansion since my main gaming group is 4 players. I also prefer the Tides of Battle cards because I like a little randomness with my battles. With the base game, I feel like it's a lot of back and forth with the nearest castles. However with the Feast for Crows expansion, then it's about secret castles that you don't where they're going for. My core group don't trust each other in any game, so we don't make as many deals as we ought to. I feel like I'm missing half of the game. I might rate this higher after a bunch more plays. I watched the TV show, but haven't read the book. It's super thematic, but with euro mechanics, which is what I like.

We don't have any similar war game in my collection as an alternative unless you count Small World which is a way lighter affair.

Things I like about aGoT: Very thematic with the house victory conditions in the Feast for Crows expansion. I kinda feel like an army general complaining about how I'm constantly short on supplies. Beautiful artwork and components. House guests get excited when they see the game in my collection.

Things I don't like about aGoT: it takes a loooong time to teach this game to new players. My core gaming group won't make deals with each other. Game length means that we have to schedule playing this game in advance. There can be some un-engaging downtime if you don't care about other people's battles.

RATING: 8.5
This game truly is the definitive Game of Thrones experience in a box.  No dice-rolling here all combat is deterministic and a mix of your units on the board and the cards you play from your hand.  The game is drenched in diplomacy, back-stabbing, and intrigue making it truly feel like you're participating in the politics of Westeros.  
I would rate this game a 9 or higher but I take points off because you truly do need the full 6 players to get the real experience.  Excellent game, however.

RATING: 9.1
Epic fantasy war game in the Game of Thrones setting. Becoming king of Westeros requires careful planning and positioning on the three influence tracks. The outcome of battles is uncertain due to the House Cards and the Tides of Battle cards. This clearly increases the excitement when going into battle. The game succeeds in having the players live the theme. It also looks very nice and contains high quality game pieces. One minor negative element? The difference between the playing pieces representing footmen, knights and siege engines could have been made more outspoken. From some angles it is difficult to distinguish between them. But hey, don't let that detail spoil your fun...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is my favorite board game.
The strategic variability on this game and level of engagement from beginning to end is on par with chess, with social political intrigue thrown in, resulting in a truly epic gaming experience. It's the ultimate hindsight game, because you always take full accountability on why you lost (or won) the game, ultimately thinking back to a series of pivotal moves that cost you the game. 
Production values are also fantastic, and for what you get it retails at a very reasonable price compared to some other board games out there.
I will say that this game isn't for everyone though, my girlfriend for instance is turned off by the level of complexity and engagement the game requires. However, those who enjoy strategic games absolutely love it. 
Also, although the game says it plays 3-6 players, playing 6 players is an absolute must - which is the reason why we don't get to play this game as often as we'd like.

Whilst a great twist on Diplomacy (I wouldn't say an upgrade, but for me it is) it suffers from similar problems; that there is little to no coming back if you fall behind. Worse, you stay in the game until it's over (which can be a long time) whilst pretty much just watching the others play. And the downtime can become a real headache if you're not involved.

tl;dr: A great game if you are ahead, a horrible game if you're behind

RATING: 4
lazy design. all balancing is done through the kingmaking (so called negotiation) aspect of the game. Houses seem imbalanced, forcing you to negotiate. Also suffers from runaway leader (compare the options of someone with just 1 unit on the map to those of someone with 8+ units on the map... who will get power more easily? who will get units more easily?). Again, this is only balanced by kingmaking. 

If you don't mind kingmaking, the game might be good for you. I don't like kingmaking at all.

Some bad iconography choices (does the sword represent killing off a unit or does it represent +1 in combat? -- in this game, it depends, where the SAME EXACT sword appears (sword token vs. combat cards) *argh*) and tacked-on-feeling rules (ports. They serve a sole purpose that's not even very easily explained thematically. Why is it that I gain power through them rather than barrels?) make me feel very doubtful about this game.

Also, this should never have been marketed as playable by 3 (there's a reason why this is not recommended with 3 players by the BGG community).

Based in the land of Westeros, the 6 houses of Westeros fight for the Iron Throne. Players try to gain control of areas which gives them varying resources, to upgrade armies and gain political powers, and expand their territories.

Heavy strategy game with fairly somewhat similar storyline for each gameplay, although final winner varies.

Fan or no, you've got to play this game at least once.

RATING: 7.44
In this version all bugs of the first Game of Thrones are fixed and the game is much largely balanced and all houses are included. Artwork remained pre-series one, and I prefer it that way.
It's great risk-like strategy game without risk. Every move can be calculated but still a lot of suspense is in the decisions when choosing orders for your armies. You can also include some randomness in combat through Tides of Battle cards as a variant for those who prefer risk and gambling. It's 6 player game, with options for less than that. I love it.
Perfect for GOT fans.
Major drawback: very long playtime with large number of players, which is at the same time best way to play it.

Typical play time is 3-4 hours

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I can see why my friends thought I would like this game.  I do, but I still think Warrior Knights is better.

Areas where GOT is better:
1-The generals/characters are flavorful/more varied, WK's are more limited in the mercenary deck (assuming you use expansion), and generals are identical for each color

2-Slightly better combat system, especially if you use the tides of war deck

3-Flavorful and easily managed supply lines dynamic

4-Never read the books/seen the show, but if I had I'm assuming I'd find it more immersive than WK's relatively generic atmosphere.

WK strengths
1-The map-I don't like how rectangular/elongated the GOT map is.  It makes me not really care about what people are doing on the other end (for example if I'm Stark, I only care about 2-3 other players).  

2-I like the win condition better in WK--I think it's dumb that strongholds/castles (whatever they're called) offer no defensive benefit.  WK *feels* like you're gaining influence over time towards being named king.

3-The specialized roles-WK WAY better.  GOT's too often only involve the player who has them.  WKs routinely affect the whole game, e.g. the Pope getting his butt kissed by other players to avoid negative events, the chairman wieding immense power at assembly, etc.

4-Having to pay your soldiers is a good balance check, I guess it could be seen as equivalent to GOT's supply lines.

I think they're even in the turn orders, and the passage of time/generic events within each turn.

Players with a love for negotiation might find something here, just never ask me to play.

RATING: 9
Just wonderful, with 3 players or more.

The concept is crudely the same that it was in Diplomacy: You have forces in your territory, then you order them to move from your territory to either attack, take hold of an unoccupied territory or move your army to defend your own area.

This is a "dice-free" game, so luck is not a factor here (unless you use the optional "Tide of Battle" cards). You have armies, comprising of infantry, knights, siege engines and ships. Above all that, you have Characters, which can give an impact to the battle itself or have some other function in some circumstances.

The beauty of this game does not lie in battles, though. Unlike in Diplomacy for example, once you have agreed to something with somebody, it's never binding; you can call off your forces right after your "ally" has entered into battle and trusts in your support!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Another case of an incredibly immerse game that goes a bit overboard in the time commitment and mechanics department; aka, a Fantasy Flight production.  The quality of the board, pieces, and artwork is fantastic, and each of the various Houses feels rather distinct, through their vastly different starting positions and unique character cards.

The purpose of the game is to have your House conquer seven strongholds on the continent of Westeros, while denying that opportunity to the other two to five other players on the increasingly crowded and war ravaged board.  The level of shifting alliances and backstabbery is quite good, which is very thematic to the story the game is based on.  And while a thorough understanding of the Song of Ice and Fire series improves the game, it definitely is not necessary to enjoy or win.

However, I feel that this game, far more than any other, including other marathon games like Arkham Horror or Merchants & Marauders, tends to drag immensely at times, and with little to do between carrying out orders, the game can easily crawl to a near standstill, making some turns far longer than necessary. And even after four complete games, it still comes off as fairly opaque strategically at times and incredibly difficult to master; while the luck factor is fairly low (no dice rolls, and few card drawing here), there doesn't always seem to be any rhyme or reason as to why one player suddenly reverses fortunes and pulls into the lead, or why other empires suddenly go into sharp decline.

Still, a good grand strategy game to play with friends who have patience and an afternoon to kill, especially to fans of war games a bit more involved than Risk, and of course of George R R Martin's epic saga.

I love that people all now want to play this game because of the TV series.

We've only played it with 4 thus far. Excited to try it with a different number of players. Love that it goes to 6.

RATING: 10
Fantástico juego que recrea con una simplicidad enorme las batallas y políticas que se reflejan en los libros de la serie Canción de Hielo y Fuego de George R.R. Martin.
Obviamente, siendo un eurogame o amerithrash, no puede descender hasta el detalle de la simulación de los ejércitos, de los lideres, de los asedios, etc..., pero con sus limitadas reglas y piezas, es capaz de crear un tenso ambiente de juego, con victorias muy ajustadas y traiciones / gestas memorables.
Además, la suerte interviene de manera muy limitada, pues todo es pura matemática (a no ser que apliques la regla opcional de avatares de la guerra) y se premia por tanto la mejor estrategía.
Muy recomendable, con el único handicap de ser necesarios 6 jugadores para que la partida alcance todo su esplendor.

RATING: 7
This game delivers an amazing war experience that feels a lot like what I imagine it would be like to live in the world of aGoT.  There's diplomacy, betrayal, armies, armadas, strategy, tactics, bidding, bluffing, and a bit of luck (although luck can be entirely removed from the game if the "Tides Of Battle" variant isn't used).  At the beginning, the game is quite simple and the turns move rather quickly.  However, as each house musters more armies and expands into new territories, the turns take longer and become more tense and mentally taxing. Very happy with this game.

I should also point out that losses and betrayals in this game feel more pointed and personal than other competitive games I've played.  I can't exactly put words to it, but the group should make a pledge to remain friends once the game ends beforehand, just to be safe.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Solid game that has a lot to offer in terms of in-depth strategy. Using your units to gain a tactical advantage on the board is fun and interesting, good bidding mechanics and the hand-management gets better the more you play. The luck factor is just big enough to make every game different and forcing you to adapt to the latest developments. Beautiful artwork and the thematic feel is amazing.

This game has a couple downsides though. You need a lot of table space, explaining all the rules takes at least half an hour (with people that are experienced in board games) and games can take hours if you play with people that want to analyze all the options, which is very frustrating if it is clear you are no longer in a winning position. Biggest downside imho is that this game will promote passive play and will rarely see good alliances, especially when played with rational calculating players. All players will converge to the same strategy, play passive for 9 turns and then go for one big final push.

RATING: 7
First game of GoT. Have been itching to try it. Overall, it is quite a solid strategy wargame. Good flavour from the source material. Good, logical flow. Simple turn structures. Easy but decision-heavy combat. A few niggly rules can bog things down at times, but rule look-ups were minimal. Waiting for the next turn can be sort of patience testing. Personally didn't like the game board art, but the card art worked very well. Sadly, this game should only be played with 6 players, so I could never justify purchasing it myself, but it's nice to know there are people out there who will play. While I give GoT an '8' for being a very solid game, it goes down a notch for being a little too grueling. The game is one of those "do the best you can with very little" and while that makes for a great thinking game, it rarely makes for a fun game. Every decision made is a decision for the best of the worst. Can be rewarding when it works, but when it doesn't it can be greatly discouraging.

RATING: 8
A very interesting multiplayer wargame.  The mechanics remind me of Dune and Diplomacy mixed. Everything works wonderfully and fits the theme perfectly.  The different armies are encouraged by the board to play a certain way, rather than the rules, which closely resemble what those armies would do in the book.  The hidden leaders during combat and the limited, secret order tokens add a  mind game element that I really enjoyed.  My only complaint is towards the middle of the game things slow down and a sort of trench warfare begins, which is thematically right, but I worry that it could become stale after a while.  With more plays I could see me rating this higher or lower.

RATING: 5
This game is beautiful and smart. The game components and art work are outstanding. The theme is strong. The game is rich. I like the fact it is deterministic, even though I miss throwing a big pile of dice, just like in Dead of Winter for instance.
It would have been great to be able to play the entire Westeros with only 4 players though. Also, the game relies heavily on war to win, and it would have been great to have other means leading to victory, like trade, better diplomacy mechanics - which would also have been quite thematic: it sometimes feels like a downgraded version of the epic Twilight Imperium.
The use of leaders when it comes to battles could have been more elegant. Also, for a 4-player game, I would have liked to be able to choose any 4 families amongst the pool of 6 on offer. Finally, the semi-cooperative aspect of the game with the wildlings threat is a good idea but could have been thought a bit more. The game never feels semi-cooperative the way Dead of Winter does for instance.
I think the games needs the expansion 'the feast for crows' to play with four players. It makes the game shorter, more varied and more punchy with the objectives that grant victory points. Same with the expansion 'dance with dragons' for 6 players.
I do enjoy playing this game because it is in the GOT universe, but it is just too back-stabbing for my group, which means we don't get to play it that often.

RATING: 9.5
One of the best area control-themed games. Newer version of Diplomacy, with more nuts & bolts. Knowing the books/show helps to enjoy the game, but is not entirely necessary. The second edition has a muted, but very nice mapboard, and the pieces are quite nice. The game can run a bit long, but is very gratifying. One of the best for this type of game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
  You are a House in Westeros, vying for power and control of the land.  You lead your armies into battle, supporting your friends and out maneuvering your enemies.  There are ten round to capture ten castles to prove your dominance.  
  This game is a where everyone must know how to play before the game begins.  It is a very deep game, though not much of a war game.  It looks like a war game, but actual battles of armies are not priority in this game.  Proof of this is that it is extremely hard to kill off troops battles, they retreat instead.  Instead this game focuses on alliances and political maneuvering to reign supreme.  There are three different powers in the game, and players bid on them in order to get special abilities.  
  This is a ton of "fun."  It is not fun while playing but after you relive it again and again.  In this way it is like RISK.  Unlike RISK, luck is almost completely negated, in and out of combat.  This means that you win or loose on your merits.  Balance is an issue however, which is a bit of a shame.
  This game is arduous to teach to new players.  It's secrecy element means they cannot learn while playing, and any weakness is immediately abused by the closest neighbors.  The game is also a lot of things to remember while playing.  As mentioned, some Houses are significantly more powerful then others in the beginning.  However they each have their own feel, which allows the players to over look this.
  A particular point of imbalance which needs to be noted is the first turn movements of Greyjoys and Lanisters.  The Greyjoys can effectively bar the Lanisters from much movement, effectively putting them back a few turns early on.
  The battle mechanics are well done, though as mentioned not exactly the focus of the game.  The game feels very much in theme to the books, and tensions rise very high in this game.  It is also very strategy based, though maybe too heavy for it's own good.  

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[i]"When you play the game of thrones, you win or you die. There is no middle ground."[/i] --Cersei Lannister describing how this game feels to play.

[b][i]Game of Thrones[/i][/b] is not a relaxing game. You will agonize over order placement while hoping you outthought your bitter rival only to be stabbed in the back by your staunchest supporter. There's little hidden information, so the game feels very cerebral. For example, suppose I'm ahead in a battle: I'm guaranteed a victory if I play (and thereby "spend") my good card. But my opponent knows this and will likely not play his good card meaning I could still win if I play a bad card. But what if he suspects I might try to save my good card and then uses his good card on the off-chance that I play loosely thereby stealing a win... you get the idea.

[b]First Edition vs Second Edition[/b]
The 2nd edition incorporated the best parts of the expansions from the 1st edition and is, to my eyes, clearly superior.

RATING: 10
My most favorite game of all time. It has deep strategic decision but not so hard to make. It's very interesting how you always start with the same starting position (for every house), but the game can play very differently according to the people on the table. For a game with no random starting location and/or map, the game has tremendous replay value according to everyone's move on round 1. The game is different every time according to the type of players on the board. Ambitious Stark makes Greyjoy defensive. Peaceful Greyjoy makes Lannister ambitious and so on. The game also has tactical decision with battle cards and temporary alliances. Play it with the right type of players and you'll love the game too!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I highly recommend this game. It's highly thematic but also works great even if you are not familiar with game of thrones. I love the mechanics this game has and especially the almost completely absence of luck. It needs a lot of negotiation and interaction between the players, thing I really appreciate in games. It's highly strategic,not only in the battlefield, where you have many possible orders for your units, but also out of it, when having to deal with 'politics' or having to 'feed' your army. The game rolls much faster after 2-3 plays, so it's not tiring at all, but really emotional and interesting. The theme as I said is awesome, making you a real Lannister or Stark etc. from the very first round.

I have played the game over 15 times so far and every game was unique. We have so much fun with my friends, when betraying or threatening each other, or when recalling lines from the books. Secretly talking to each other in order to attack or defence from someone break 'alliances' is really glorifying the game.

The only disadvantage in this game I can find (which is not actually a big one) is that it works perfect only with 6 players. I have tried playing with 3,4,5 players, but it doesn't work good at all. Neutral forces being placed with less than 6 players are easily defeated most times, making the player next to them really overpowered and allowing him to expand really fast.

Overall it's a 'must' game for game of thrones fans, who like strategy in and out the battlefield. If you have a 6 player group that likes games like this, pick it up and have fun!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Worst game I've played. It comes down to a combination of things:

- game length: first game took all afternoon plus a majority of the evening. Other times it took around 5-6 hours to finish. 

- complexity: this is not an easy game to teach or understand.

- experienced players have a big edge: the game is 'figured out' to the point that there are opening moves for different houses. If you don't open with one of these moves your essentially playing the game sub-optimal.

- (pseudo) player elimination: you can get wiped out, or more commonly, get beat to the point of no return. First game I played someone started playing Starcraft 2 because he had almost nothing left after a couple of turns. I quite the last game that I played after 3/4 rounds because there was no coming back.

- the diplomacy aspect: I'm not a fan of this in general, but in GoT it was the worst because of the Lannister and Greyjoy starting positions. You had to strike a truce from turn 1, or else you couldn't win. 

All of these things combined made for a terrible gaming experience. I don't mind most of these aspects individually. But when put together it made for a long and stressful experience that gave me no enjoyment. A Game of Thrones isn't an objectively bad board game. It's just a game that I never want to play again and almost turned me off boardgaming all together.

RATING: 8
Great adaptation of the source material. The action system is fantastic, where you place a face down order per province you control. Once all provinces have one, then you flip them over and resolve. This can create really interesting situations where you agree to do something but end up doing the opposite - just like in the books.

RATING: 7
As a first foray into multiplayer negotiated wargaming, it is a good game because it (a) is true to the books; (b) is not so complicated; and (c) promotes some negotiation.

But it is not the most interesting of its genre. Dune is far better, possibly because AGOT suffers from a too much determinism. At the very least, there are optimal pathways for initial plays (gambits) and overall strategies.

It's possible that it will enjoy a cult-like following for years that could engender institutional strategy that will give it a legacy. Or it could go the way of the do-do.

Somehow I suspect that Fief: France 1429 will be a better game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.5
Although there are some not too common mechanics, it felt like a pretty standard conquest game to me, typical FFG's bloated design with nothing particularly good, interesting or even worth a second play.

Players stay around their home regions for most of the game, trying to get and keep castles. The game moves slowly, because, for the most part, you depend on a random event in order to recruit new troops. Combat is based on cards, not dice, but since the cards are open information, it takes longer than it should, as players check all the values, special powers and possible interactions. My only game too often came to a halt when combat happened. Maybe I was just bored with the rest of the game already, but the downtime during other players’ battles was excruciatingly painful. At least I wasn’t Lannister, because the game seems heavily unbalanced against it, and that player was clearly out of contention very early. The game lasted some five hours, and after such an overly long time, it was frustrating to see it end in a bash-the-leader fest, with potential for kingmaking, until someone sneaked in a victory.

On the production, I don’t understand why FFG did not upgrade the units to miniatures in this second edition. At first, the pieces look sufficiently distinguishable, but may be a little confusing and hard to tell at a glance on the board.

RATING: 4
I used to like A Game of Thrones a lot. But after a few bad plays, I'm lowering my rating quite a bit. The Westeros decks are so arbitrary. When the cards come out in the right order, the game is very satisfying, even if I am on the losing end of the order. But when the cards come out in any other way, the game seems to come down to who benefits from the arbitrary order of Westeros cards.

Furthermore, the game is really decided after 6 rounds. No one can realistically win via the auto-win condition. So why have the game last an extra four game rounds? If you like the game that much, play two sessions of it to 6 rounds instead of one game of it to ten.

RATING: 7
Thème intéressant. Les figurines sont difficilement différenciables. Le jeu reste un jeu d'élimination où des joueurs affaiblis peuvent difficilement remonter la pente et même se faire éliminer. Les joueurs qui ont une force d'armée moins forte peuvent attendre un temps assez long pour que les joueurs les plus forts finissent leur tour. Cela enlève l'intérêt du jeu pour tous les joueurs et le jeu peut parraître alors assez long. Le jeu à quelques défauts au niveau de l'esthétisme. On est loin de l'esthétisme d'un Cyclade.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
This epic game of war and intrigue gets your right into the thick of it by turn 2. No holds barred above and beyond the ones you painfully negotiate with your neighbouring houses (at great cost). This game moves at a reasonable pace by keeping each and every action turn-based or simultaneous so you never wait too long. The secret order laying is especially clever. Furthermore, this game captures much of the flavour of the much loved book or television series in terms of setting up the asymmetrical tensions, abilities, and mechanisms for supporting and betraying each other.

This is certainly an easy step up from RISK while still feeling very meaningful.

RATING: 9
Game of Thrones: The Board Game's mechanics do an excellent job of encouraging players to battle and interact. RATING: 9
(01/2012) Played a friend's new second edition copy. I already own the first edition with both expansions, so I will comment on the differences.

The game or gameplay have not changed significantly, although there are a few minor touches and tweaks that are noteworthy:
1) Board is striking in appearance, with gorgeous artwork, starker colors, and more medieval look. However, the board is much thinner than the 1st edition (think AH Civilization quality, versus the board quality of the latest crop of games like Brass), and it has a very noticeable (and not immediately correctable) warp; is FFG trying to cut corners here? On the other hand, the counters look like they popped out of the counter sheets cleanly with very little flare/fuzz, compared to the 1st edition. Cards have nice linen finish, typical of latest FFG, instead of matt finish from 1st edition. Units are now made of equally gorgeous colored marbled plastic. Players now have player aid screens. Board now has a victory point track, whereas before you just had to count and remember where everyone was at.
2) More dynamic events in the Westeros cards (from Storm of Swords expansion), some giving the Iron Throne, Valyrian Sword, and the Messenger Raven owners something to choose from a menu of three possible events.
3) More neutral tokens (instead of the original three) to help adjust (and provide obstacles) for less than six players. Game comes ready to play with six (from Clash of Kings expansion), with House Martel included.
4) Ports, siege engines, and garrisons are now part of the main game (from Clash of Kings expansion), and they integrate seamlessly.
5) There is a new Wildlings event deck (from Storm of Swords), which gives a unpredictable twist to what happens if the Night Watch wins or loses. Now, the Raven owner can peek at an upcoming card. Before, the rewards/consequences were deterministic.
6) Two of the special Orders have expanded capabilities--Consolidate power will now allow collecting a power token OR mustering if at city/stronghold; Raid can now cancel a defense order (before, would cancel two raid/support/consolidate orders). These capabilities were specific to Special One-Time Order tokens from two different Houses in Storm of Swords.
7) The House cards have more interesting, dangerous capabilities than from the 1st edition (from Storm of Swords expansion). For example, the #3 House Tyrell card allows the attacker, if he/she wins the battle, to keep the attack order token with the attacking force! (This allowed me to sweep through two areas to reach an unsupported 7th city for the win.)

All in all, this is a very impressive 2nd edition, taking the best elements from the Clash of Kings and Storm of Swords expansions. The rules are much clearer and concise than the 1st edition, with a lot of loop holes and potential questions cleared up with examples. The production quality is significantly better, such that it makes me want to get the 2nd edition, if it weren't for the huge miss with the board quality/thickness. Still, a good solid game that has finally "settled". Initial rating an '8'.

Update (02/26/12) I am almost tempted to get the 2nd edition, just for the artwork. I am finally sold on the marbled plastic pieces too. I am convinced that it has hit upon the perfect blend of variant rules from the two expansions; it doesn't need anything else to improve it. It is on its way to becoming "great", but needs more plays. Upping rating to a '9'. However, my friend's board continues to warp even more. :shake:

Other than that, I have no complaints about the game! Go buy it! It's seriously freaking awesome!

OWNED SINCE 2011
    This is a great game. It is fast, easy to learn, and the mechanics of the game usually keeps the game close until the end; no one every wins by a land slide, and no one can monopolize the board. Would recommend to any one, even non- hard core gamers. 
    My kids, ages 7 years and older, can play this one just fine. Even my younger one, who does not take everything too serious can have fun. If nothing else he likes to attack and "bother" people. When he does this the game is still fun, and because his behavior is unpredictable it is a great strategy in and of itself. The parents and children enjoy this game, but definitely best with 6 players unless you are playing with one of the 4 player expansion scenarios. Overall, a good game that becomes great when everyone playing competitively to win.
