RATING: 2
Played this for the first time the other night, and it will hopefully be my last. 

This game hits on practically every hallmark of an unpleasant game experience. Let's keep a tally shall we?

- Incredibly steep learning curve and overly complex rule system making it overwhelmingly unfriendly to beginners and difficult for veterans to explain adequately? Check
- Over an hour just to take the first game turn (mostly due to rules explanation)? Check
- The need to know and understand every facet of the game's minutiae from the very first turn in order to properly execute any sort of competent strategy? Check
- Cumbersome combat and unit instruction mechanic? Check
- Imbalanced (VERY imbalanced) factions? Check
- Not well designed to be played with anything less than the maximum amount of players? Check
- Game length should be expected to exceed 5 hours? Check
- Horrible down time between turns? Check
- A faux-"perfect information" game which actually has an alarming amount of hidden information and luck (via card abilities, game events, action limitations, etc.)? Check
- Certain players never interacting with one another in a 4+ hour game due to mechanics and layout? Check
- The most clear and viable strategy (really the ONLY strategy it seems) being to lay low and avoid conflict and interaction, hoping for other players to beat each other up? Check
- "Take that" style of gameplay being the overwhelming draw? Check
- Due to previous point, the overwhelming feeling everyone experiences is animosity on a night that is supposed to be fun? Check
- Irrelevant theme in a game where bad game mechanics exist in order to facilitate the supposed theme? Check
- Uninteresting decisions? Check
- Forced actions (often with no meaning)? Check
- Little to no tactical opportunities? Check
- Potential for players to be effectively eliminated in the first turn or so? Check
- Heavy dose of kingmaking due to the previous point? Check
- Runaway leader problems which can only be resolved by people ganging up on the leader? Check
- Does not allow nor encourage creative paths to victory? Check
- No incentive for conflict other than a lack of anything better to do? Check
- Impressively shallow gameplay considering time commitment required? Check
- Nothing inherently fun in the primary game mechanic (selecting action tokens)? Check

This game was an absolute chore in all the negative ways the word "chore" implies. It wasn't fun, it was long, it was arduous, and in the end it felt like all the back and forth and give and take mattered for naught. Essentially we could have accomplished the same experience had we just sat around the table and argued over who we wanted to win: 

Person 1: "I think Person 5 should win" 
Person 2: "but I'm about to win..." 
Person 1: "yeah, but she almost quit earlier, and if she won now that would be cool" 
Person 3: "yeah, and I'm not going to win anyway, and I don't want you to win, so I'll just say let's let Person 5 win"
Person 4: "Yeah, Person 5 wins"
Person 2: "Darn..."
Person 5: "W00t"

Playing this game feels like playing Tic-Tac-Toe for hours, but instead of draws, you argue over who wins the draw and get a friend to settle the argument one way or the other. Over, and over, and over. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
171
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Another game where the best way to win is to gather your forces and wait for others to show weakness make and horrible mistakes.


Edit 2017:

I do like the marbled plastic player pieces and the production values of the rest is not far behind. I guess that I mainly object to the incredibly claustrophobic feel of the map as well as the feeling that the winning strategy seems to be waiting for moments of weakness on other players parts rather you managing to build anything yourself. 

I seem to prefer Forbidden Stars as that atleast gives you little something to build and upgrade. This just feels too tight and merciless for a game of this length. Also knowing all the combat decks by heart offers a rather impressive advantage. Another minus on my part is that a win is predicated on holding the castles and as such to win you have to advance and hold ground. This does not leave room for other sneaky play than sit and build and explode when others are weak. 

Perhaps I've just played with meh company but that is pretty much the only way I see this working in the mid and late game. Early game can be interesting in the expansion and beginning conflict shuffle. Not too enjoyable for me though because of the vacillary nature of map states. You just never feel like you've achieved much until you get to a pouncing distance. Also playing really well pretty much guarantees that you have to grind someones face with your boot to have the room needed unless the others have played poorly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I love this game. It has some great mechanics that work very well: the action planning phase is very good, the idea of assigning actions in secret to every army on the board and the option of removing orders played by the other players, the 3 trackers that change by betting with influence tokens, I think that it is a very good idea that does tokens are used as well for controlling territories without an army. I think that everything works amazingly fine. Even the game shouldn't be too long. The longest phase is planning at the beginning of each turn, after that, the resolution should be fast (it should, there are always slow players). The combat mechanics is really interesting but introduces one thing that may be an issue, which is that every player should know every army's deck.
Positive:
1. The mechanics. Work very well
2. The back and forth structure of the game. If every player works reasonably well, it is very difficult for every player to obtain a total victory before the end of the last turn
3. The crown, raven... trackers
4. The unique card decks of every house
Negative:
1. This game has to be played by 6 players. Period. The recommended houses configurations for 3, 4 or 5 players do not work. Even alternative setups are not very good. For 6 is perfect
2. Every player needs to play well. If Highgarden doesn't protect the south area from Sun Spear, that player will win. Without any doubt. It is mandatory, every border on the map has to be at stake during the whole game
3. The sea is extremely powerful. Controlling the sea means everything. Particularly for some houses like Baratheon and Greyjoy
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
Sort of a weird game, I was the Baratheons and it seemed like I was very heavily incentivized to attack the Lannisters, but that went poorly because the battle outcome is determined semi-randomly. So I was down to like 3 troops on the board very quickly, and by chance no "get more guys" end of round cards came up for a while. But then I don't know what happened really because the Lannisters and Arryns sort of just had minor squabbles with each other and the Starks were left pretty much alone in the north for a while, and so they just destroyed the rest of us. I saw it happening but I didn't want to say anything because I figured I had no shot and I certainly didn't want the Lannisters to win at that point. But in spite of my particular experience sounding pretty unpleasant, I could see an interesting game in there. For one picking your action discs simultaneously is pretty interesting, though the fact that you could just never have any stars all game is pretty brutal (probably not insurmountable, just unfun). Also the special effects of the cards you play to supplement your troops are a cool nod to the characters themselves and add some interesting strategic decisions. The best part though is probably that the deck of cards that randomly adjust the battle outcomes are apparently only for beginners (in particular they're optional) so we can just never use those again. Overall I liked it about as much as I think I could like an Ameritrashy DoaM game, which is just enough that I'd be ok playing this if someone else really wanted to.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is very mucb an Amerigame in which you control Houses from Game of Thrones and seek to dominate Westeros.  For fans of the series, it does a good job of evoking the original material.  You have to maneuver to capture provinces that in turn provide you with various kinds of resources.  A key aspect is that you put a face-down marker on each province that indicates what you will be doing and they are then flipped simultaneously.  This allows for plenty of negotiations and potential back stabs followed by a dramatic reveal.  There is also periodic bidding over positioning on one of three powerful tracks that determine how moves first, who wins ties, and so forth.  Winning requires making alliances and then turning on your ally at the right time.  I'm not a big fan of Diplomacy type games so I'm not giving it a high rating but it's a very good implementation.  I can be talked into playing it usually despite my aversion to this type of game play.

RATING: 4.5
2013-05-21

Inital Rating: 7.0 (April 2012)

What a disappointment. I thought the game was going to be great as I love negotiation games and the fantasy setting of A Game of Thrones. Perhaps my bad experience was due to my game group but now I almost always resist a game of this.  

The game explanation can take a while but the play time is the worst, it can be very high with lots of downtime (very repetitive), the game advances very slowly, I feel very restricted in my decisions and I´m obliged to fight with my neighbors no matter what. Also there seems to be a lot of unbalance between factions, apparently you can’t play this with fewer than 6 players. It’s like a worse Risk. 

When I play this I feel like I’m wasting my precious boardgaming time. I’m downgrading its rating to a level I feel matches my feelings toward the game.

On the good side the game production values are pretty nice, although  I would have preferred miniatures to show the armies on the map.

In conclusion, a bad tedious game. I will give it another chance in the far future, but if the mood strikes to play something like this, I will always prefer Dune over A Game of Thrones.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I think the published 2nd Edition is probably better than the 1st Edition Core Game but probably worse than the combined 1st Ed + 2 Expansions. The biggest "difference" is the addition of the "kicker" cards and I don't think I like them.  The game is pretty much built on tight combat results and letting Support Orders and Fiefdoms Track ranking determine the outcome.  As you add more randomness, the outcomes are less deterministic and it becomes more of a "Dudes on a Map" Game.

I've long held to the belief that this game benefits from less rules, not more, and I usually opted to play 1st Edition without alot of the more byzantine rules but this includes them as basic rules.  Overall, it's not as simple to explain to new players and less flexible to expand than the 1st Edition.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played 2 sessions this weekend with 4 players. Essentially this game reminded me of a more robust version of Risk set in Westeros. I did enjoy the plays.  I particularly enjoyed the asymmetry of the game. Each house starts with different strategies to put in place based on their starting location and units, and unique house cards for use in battle.  I also liked how each troop (or army) on the board received unique orders. 

The simultaneous placement of troop orders did work to move the game along . . .  but our games nevertheless, lasted 6 hours and 7 hours respectively (there were short breaks for cooking, eating, and beverages in between - also the first game involved some set up and rules time)- BEWARE THIS IS A LONG GAME.  Interestingly, our second game when we already had the rules down actually took longer than the first (understanding the moves and other players hands etc. resulted in longer analysis time!). 

The game is by nature aggressive, some houses have to be more aggressive than others based on where they are situated but combat is an essential part of the game - so for those who don't like aggressive games -- stay away from this one.  Personally, aggressive games aren't my favorite but I don't exclude them categorically and since this game is premised on battling and area control everyone knows what they are getting into.  

All-in-all this is a unique game experience and was fun - but in retrospect, if someone told me I could have 12 hours of gaming I'm not sure I would want to spend it on two games of GOT.

RATING: 7
I held back buying the first edition because of a bad playing experience, but after re-reading the books I decided to grab a copy of the second edition.

Some of the problems I had with the first edition are still there and the game is very prone to AP, but this edition appears to be better organised.

Although deal-making is really never mentioned in the rules, you cannot win the game on your own.  Deals must be made (and broken) if you want to win.  Herein lies my biggest complaint... diplomacy can drag out the game to an exruciating length.  The two to four hours listed on the box can easily extend to six or more.

The 2nd edition components are worth the price.  Gone are the bright colours and wooden pieces of the original.  The new map has been redrawn with darker shades and the new plastic pieces are fantastic.  Everything has a grittier 'feel'... much like the books the game is trying to represent.  My rating has increased a notch because of this.

I know that I'll probably never play this game more than one or two times, but I'm glad to have it in my collection.

RATING: 8
A veces cuando juegas a un juego basado en una saga (pelis, series de TV, libros...) te das cuenta de que lo crearon sólo por sacar provecho pero el juesgo está vacío. Ése no es el caso de JdT, en mi opinión.

Lo primero de todo: el tema encaja perféctamente con la mecánica elegida. Una lucha continua por el poder y el territorio es lo que puedes encontrar en JdT.

El núcelo del juego es la asignación secreta de órdenes a tus unidades. Esto no es ninguna revolución, si alguna vez has jugado al clásico juego de mesa "Diplomacy" ya has visto este sistema. Es inteligente, elegante y no hay tiempos muertos porque todo el mundo juega a la vez.

Resolver las órdenes es tambien rápido. En esta fase los jugadores podrían entrar en combate y jugar sus "chulísimas" cartas de personaje, dependiendo de la casa que hayan elegido.

Hay más cosas en el juego, como la fase de los Westeros, los diferentes tipos de marcadores... pero, como he dicho antes, las órdenes son lo más importante.

En un juego como JdT el acabado visual debe de ser impresionante y pienso que este juego lo tiene. Las cartas son muy bonitas, el tablero es grande y lleno de detalles... pero las piezas son simplemente excelentes. No son de madera sino de plástico pero son muy molonas.

Resumiendo, JdT es un juego muy bueno en el que puedes elegir tu casa favorita basada en la novela y pelear contra tus amigos por el control del Trono de Hierro.
[color=green]
LAST PLAY = 2013
[/color][color=blue]
Sometimes when you play a game based on a saga (films, TV shows, books...) you notice that they created it only to get profit of its name but the game itself is empty. That's not what you get with GoT IMHO.

First of all: its theme fits perfect with the mechanic chosen. A continuous struggle for power and territory is what you can find in GoT.

The main core of the game is assigning secretly orders to your units. This is not a revolution, if you ever have played to the classic boardgame "Diplomacy" you have already seen this system. It´s clever and elegant and there is no downtime because everybody is playing at the same time.

Resolving orders is also fast. In this phase players could get into combat and play their "cool" character's cards, depending on the House they chose.

There are also other things in the game, like the Westeros phase, the different kind of tracks... but, as I said before, the orders are the most important.

In a game like GoT the art must be impressive and I think this game owns it. Cards are very beautiful, board is big and plenty of details... but tokens are simply excellent. No wood but plastic but they are pretty cool.

Summarizing, GoT is a very good game where you can choose your favorite novel-based house and fight against your friends for the control of the Iron Throne.[/color]

RATING: 4

RATING: N/A
In the second edition of A Game of Thrones: The Board Game, three to six players take on the roles of the great Houses of the Seven Kingdoms of Westeros, as they vie for control of the Iron Throne through the use of diplomacy and warfare. Based on the best-selling A Song of Ice and Fire series of fantasy novels by George R.R. Martin, A Game of Thrones is an epic board game in which it will take more than military might to win. Will you take power through force, use honeyed words to coerce your way onto the throne, or rally the townsfolk to your side? Through strategic planning, masterful diplomacy, and clever card play, spread your influence over Westeros!

RATING: 5.5
So much asymmetry in factions (battle cards) that players are required to study the differences between the factions (especially combat decks) before being able to make intelligent strategic plays, a requirement that I found to be a tedious burden.

So much asymmetry in board position (geography/layout/positioning of board objectives) that perceiving the relative board-advantage/lead of players is difficult and mentally taxing, if not impossible. 

Overall, it's Ameritrash very much in the vein of of Axis & Allies. The only reason I'm willing to play is that there is no dice-rolling or RNG.

Action programming is well-implemented.

RATING: 7
[u]Comments:[/u]
A good update to the original game. There are a few minor quibbles that I have. I really like the Leaders from the second expansion from the first edition, and this edition does not include those. I also REALLY do not like the changes to the raid orders that is present in this version. I do however like the additional mustering rule with the consolidate power orders. Having the ports preprinted on the board is VERY helpful, and the decks are all well balanced. Overall a good addition to the Game of Thrones world.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I have long enjoyed the first edition of this game and you can read my comments on that edition for my explanation why.  This version adds some interesting elements from expansions and some new twists.  The ports are a necessary addition - in fact the original version is almost unplayable without ports in my opinion (though one can use port rules without actually having port pieces).  The siege engines are a good addition as well because they make the game less static with their outsized offensive benefit and defense liabilities.  I am neutral on the starred consolidate power token's muster feature.  The wilding cards seem to add an interesting element.  I prefer the old house cards as they make battles less unpredictably swingy (although that could be a benefit if you really know the decks or like a little more chaos).  I have played this version with 5 and find the original board far more balanced for that number of players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
A very good system of a game that fall flat in gameplays.

Its too long for what it is. You have too little actions in the game yet what takes it too long are the book-keeping, negotiating and "actual" combat resolve.

This is my real complain for this game. The negotiation part is acceptable. Its the core mechanic of this game and it would not be the same game otherwise. The book-keeping and resolvation of orders/combat are what really takes too long. 

Imagine thinking about taking 5mins depending on the amount of diplomacy and negotation for your turn to place your orders then having to wait 15-30mins until you get to play again, frustrating is that especially when you dont do any fighting. The combat part also takes too long... to count up the numbers and announce it to people, to negotiate and finally play your cards... far far too long.

(This game could really do well in a digital format, where computer do the book-keeping for you to streamline the experience and shorten the ansolutely boring long waiting time)

The tide of war cards also let the game down with the fact that even the most well laid plan could be twarted, foiled and devastated by random chance.
(But it could very well be removed from that game.)

There are no catch up mechanisms in the game. Once you fall, you fall hard and can only sit there doing random things that don't matter to the gamestate anymore.

The game is rather unfair for some house in a game with less thant he maximum number of people. Some house can easily "farm" the neutral houses in that regard where the others cannot do so as easily.

However, despite a long of complain coming from my ipad, i could point out the very unique flavour of the Game of Throne (books and series) being very much the soul of this game. The designer team surely translate well the spirit of the franchise into the game in a well thought manner.

However, the game itself surely suffer from the actual gameplay that takes too long between turns and various other things.

A digital version of this game would surely be one heck of a game to play but on the analog and manual cardboards"?

Its a nono for me.

RATING: 8
I didn't do well in my first game. It was a 6-player game and I was Tyrell who was stuck in a very bitter war against Martell. The Martell player was very weak in that he lost every war against me (he wasn't a good player) and he was also very vindictive as he was unwilling to agree to a truce and just wanted to crush my house (pathetic effort). It was very easy to defend against him but my resources were tied up from round 1 itself so I found it very difficult to expand while Stark, Baratheon and Greyjoy was having a field day coming down south. My point is, this game is very thematic and even though I wasn't doing well, I felt that the tension and frustration happening around the table was very true to each of the houses. However I would love to play this game with better players the next time. :) I'm going to mark this an 8 for now with good potential to go up to 9.

RATING: 9
You take on the role of one of the great Houses of Westeros.  You can build armies and fleets but are limited by supply.  The combat is card based.  Success is less about Risk-like constant attack and more about maneuver and knowing when to strike for the win.  

There is a heavy dose of diplomacy as well.  

All six houses are represented in this edition.  It has excellent balance with each player count having neutral armies to act as a balancing mechanism.  It is still hard for Lannister to survive.  

Play time can run very long if players do not play combat quickly.  If the playtime was not so long this game would be a 10.

RATING: 4
This game just screams unbalanced and random.  That's fine for a filler but painful for a game that can last five or six hours. This game can sometimes be very tight for most of the play time duration, until the last couple rounds when a random card draw allows someone to pull ahead of the pack. Or a game could have a couple runaway leaders because some Houses have an army advantage from the start and if the random card draws don't balance this out then the other players will never be able to catch up, resulting in a king-making situation.

For me, this all means that those first three to four hours don't have any real affect on the game and/or some players may have to sit through them knowing that they have no chance of winning. You are at the mercy of the decks, which could be forced to reshuffle over and over before you ever see a card that helps you. For the most part, I like the mechanisms and interactions, but in a 5- or 6-player game, one or two players are guaranteed to have no chance and will be forced to sit at the table for hours on end. That's just a bad design.

RATING: 10
An amazing game. It takes the central mechanic of Diplomacy (area control and alliance building), but adds in aGoT theme, a better combat mechanic, and also simplifying all aspects it and cutting time. 

The fun is you're limited with only so many actions (attack, pillage, support, or resource generation). You cant do all you want, which pushes you toward alliance making. Game of Thrones theme shines by claiming titles to gain bonuses, and the threat of Wildlings who if can weaken your forces. The goal is simple, control 10 castles, which evoke RISK, but again, alliance making, and back-stabbing are needed. There are no dice and no luck (besides some card draws), so the game puts you in charge of your own fate. With so much going on, however, it is not a light game at all.  

Purchased: 05/30/16 (MiniatureMarket)

RATING: 9
It's more than a war game, more than resource acquisition, more than diplomacy: the second edition of A Game of Thrones is very tightly designed, with straightforward game play.

It's a Fantasy Flight game, but it lacks this developer's typical byzantine rules and baroque mechanics. It's actually clean and intuitive. Particularly welcome is the lack of a distracting and heavily abstract phase of "noodling about," which so often appears in Fantasy Flight productions to destroy the flow.

Clean, sharp, intuitive, and extremely fun, this game is a must-have, even if you don't give a whit for the background material.

Finally, while many critics complain about the length, our group of 6 got through its first play in 3 hours, without rushing. If it's taking your group 7 hours, that would be boring . . . then again, it's probably you, not the game. 

We fully expect to have this one down to 2-2.5 hours after a few plays.

RATING: 4
I love A Song of Ice and Fire. The mechanics of this game are good but they don't match the theme at all. I'd simplistically describe the mechanics as "better Risk", allowing for more diplomacy and tension. However, the core idea of the rules remains, as in Risk, expanding your territories across a map. This isn't representative of A Song of Ice and Fire: in the books the houses don't expand their territories then grind against one another at the borders. Furthermore, the game requires the players to self-balance it through diplomacy, doesn't work for anything less than 6 players and, rather than building to an exciting climax, just ends after turn 10. It's a good rules system but a poor game. For how to adapt a beloved fantasy book into a good board game, see War of the Ring.

RATING: 1
This game makes me want to start punching people as soon as it hits the table. No other games does that.

I'll give you an example of a set of rules that I think feels the same as this game:
- Each player writes "rock," "paper," or "scissors" on a piece of paper.
- Each player then chooses either the player to his left or to his right to be his target.
- The pieces of paper are then revealed.
- Sit around a move a bunch of plastic and cardboard for 20 minutes.
- If you beat your target, you get 1 point. If someone else targeted you and beat you, you get -1 point.
- If you get -1 points 2 turns in a row, you lose, but you can't leave the table.
- Repeat these steps until all but one person has lost or it has been done 10 times.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Houserules:

Setup:
Lannister starts with 1 footman and a +5 garrison in King's Landing.

Rules:
Only 1 ship from each sea zone can support a land battle.

Garrisons are not units and can't place orders.

When fighting neutrals, both sides use a house card (random card for neutrals) and both get a TOB card. After battle, neutral gets card house card back.

Keeps give +1, Castles give +2.

Iron throne can pass on first action. Determines all non-combat ties (including game end).

Valerian Steel Blade can let you draw a 2nd TOB card (keep the best one) instead of the +1 bonus at the end of battle.
If used to support another battle, it must be declared as part of the support phase.

Raven lets you look at any one upcoming event card and switch an order token with one from your supply or another one from the board.

You can always scuttle one of your own units at anytime during the game.

Remember: Wildlings start at 2. When retreating, you may only retreat to one (legal) zone. Routed units can still defend (but at power 0). If defeated again, they die (since routed units can't retreat).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5

Must play with 6 players only!

I also use the T6 setup and card changes from DWD expansion.

RATING: 6
Strong thematic game. It plays a bit like Risk, but without the dice. Say what? It has the hidden simultaneous move mechanics and it's basically a war game about using resources to build an army to win, but without dice there is absolutely no randomness and winning relies more on your strategy and negotiating skills -- the game rewards players who can bluff, negotiate and betray their opponents to get favorable board position.

The main drawback of this game is that it is slow, cumbersome, requires way too much bookkeeping and takes some really dedicated time to play. The players also need to absorb a lot of complexity as the mechanics are not very streamlined, but this gives the game depth at least.

RATING: 9
Use your armies to expand your lands, armies and power to control most victory areas in Westeros.

Plays: More than 50.
Type: Thematic Wargame = a theme-driven game where you move military forces on a map to destroy other military forces and/or conquer territory. Area movement style. Strategic scale.
Complexity: Medium wargame.
# of players: 6.
Playing time: 300 mins.
Player interaction: Conflict and Diplomacy.
Randomness: Low – card draw, but cards have global effects that you can prepare for.

Replayability: Very high. The assymetrical starting positions just beg for repeated plays to explore the strategies of the different houses. Every game will be different depending on the diplomatic balance. There is also depth to explore.
Fun: I’m a big fan of multi-player wargames in general and of simultaneous order selection in particular, and this is a home run for me. So much fun with diplomacy and backstabbing, and the perfect theme to go with such a game. Even more fun now that more people know the backstory from the TV-series.
Memorability: Very high. You’ll definitely remember the alliances made and broken and the shifting fortunes of war. The theme shines here as well, making it sound like you’re part of the novels (or the TV-series) when you regale your friends with the tale of your latest victory.

Thematic integration: Very high.
Components: Very good. The map of Westeros is gorgeous and the pieces and chits are of good quality with good art.

Who will like this: Gamers that want a multi-player wargame need look no further. The theme is icing on the cake for those that like it, but you don’t need to to enjoy the game.

RATING: 5
Finally got to play this one and I was honestly pretty disappointed.  I really wanted to like it.  We played a 6 player game with 5 new players and it took about 6 hours (plus teaching).  That's just too long for what it is.  I spent a large part of that time without a lot to do.  I played Tyrel (Green) and felt like they had a really tough starting position.  There's just enough rules to be pretty confusing most of the game.  There were a few things I had to ask about nearly every turn.  Just didn't feel very intuitive to me.  The actions really felt like a big rock/paper/scissors game which I often enjoy in lighter fare but not in a game of this length.  I guessed wrong a lot.  Several times when I finally built up enough troops to try to attack somewhere I got screwed by a card even though I had the greater forces.  It's what seems typical of Fantasy Flight Games.  Great art and production, but I just don't enjoy what I like to call "the whammy cards."  I guess once you get to know the game well enough you can try to plan around them, but I honestly don't think I care enough to play this several times.  We didn't play up the negotiation much and that might improve and balance the game but I just didn't enjoy it enough the first time.  For supposedly having such a luck-less game, it sure felt chaotic to me.  Blind bidding.  Rock/Paper/Scissors style combat, both in the order playing, and the combat resolution.  It's not what I call strategy to have to guess what the other person is going to do.  Fun in a shorter game.  Perhaps that's where negotiation comes in. But it's gonna be a long while before I feel up to giving this another chance I'm afraid.

RATING: 8
I love 'em cutthroat.  The betrayal is superb as long as the players involved can be flexible enough to know that when it's done, it's not personal.  I think this is a great game to roleplay your faction and when everyone is on board, it's a blast!  That being said, one person who takes it personal or doesn't understand the betrayal mechanic can sour the whole experience.

My gripe is minor: there's so much that goes onto the board that it can be overwhelming sometimes, especially in 5 or more player games.  I kind of like the stress but I see it crack others because they get that analysis paralysis and make mistakes.  I just take it in stride.  You don't get a free pass beyond the pearly gates just because you won a game.  Sit back and enjoy.  If you're overwhelmed, simplify your actions & betray.  It makes for great surprises.

RATING: 7
+.5 has depth and a feeling of balancing lots of things...but also has a weight and complexity to match
+1 various paths to victory work well
+1 several mechanics (forced cooperation, encouraged scheming/plotting, and bidding for powers) add a good social/bluffing element. 
+1 Combat feels high-stakes and dangerous; and units feel valuable due to limited army size, infrequent recruiting and delayed reveal of actions/counter-actions
+.5 artwork / theme done well


-.5 Very unbalanced...but this is mitigated somewhat by the plotting/backstabbing that results, which in itself feels very game of thrones-y. (Bug or feature?)

-1 rule complexity was more than enough to put at least one of my groups off giving it a second run-through, and the rule book could've been a lot better. 

A great big-box wargame for exactly 6 weighty-game lovers to sink their teeth into. I have a feeling my ratings of this game may change if I get the right group around it.

RATING: 2.5
One of my greatest boardgame disappointments. A game where the very mechanics allow players to fight together against the winner, who got ahead by his strategy on his own, seems a lot unfair - and that's far from the only unfair mechanics. For example, in the supplies, it doesn't really matter AT ALL how much supplies you hold in the long term, or how much you gained or lost - it only matters in the extremely rare and totally unpredictable moments, when the supplies are calculated and updated.
Plus, later turns rather drag on, with all the units that need their orders.
The opening setup is heavily unbalanced, maybe in lieu of the story, but since I am a fan of board games and not of the GoT series, my total opinion is "no, thanks".

Plus, the only board game that GUARANTEES some heavy and evil quarrels among the players.

RATING: 6
Quien es fanático de Juego de Tronos puede sentir que este juego se acerca a lo que es ese factor de no saber quien está de tu lado o en contra de la serie (libros y tv). Pero, para lograrlo emplea mecánicas de juego que no necesariamente son fáciles de manejar, está diseñado para jugadores duros. 
Otro aspecto, es que se siente mucho mejor con los 6 jugadores pero ahí lleva a que esa ese tipo de juegos que dura muchísimo. Claramente no es un TI, donde tienes n factores que sientes que desde el inicio al fin tu imperio ha crecido notablemente (tecnologías, estrategias, tropas, etc.), aquí terminas con los mismos elementos con los que empezó el juego, así que al final de 3 horas, ganaste pero sin sentirte muy superior a los demás, más que controlar terreno. Ojo, para Juego de Tronos, no es necesario controlar otros factores más que terreno y ser el amo del Trono de Hierro, pero luego de tantas horas invertidas me gustaría ver crecer a los personajes, tal como los hijos Stark en la serie.
Fuera de eso, juego sólido, momentos épicos en las batallas, recomendable para un grupo de personas con tiempo y que amen a GoT (que ahora hay muchos!)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Game of Thrones is an uneven area-control war-game that wishes it was more of a diplomacy game then it actually is.

Its very long (always 3+ hours), most of which is spent grinding against adjacent opponents over the same mutually neighbouring spaces. The winning condition will almost never be met, and most games will finish when the game ends after 10 long rounds.

The combination of secret actions, restricted army building and remaining combat ability being open knowledge makes the game incredibly unforgiving.

Players will attack each other through paranoia, alliances are difficult to make as other players will gang up on the alliance, and with awkward number restrictions on army pieces you can often be outnumbered and overwhelmed from several sides at once, whilst at the same time always feeling like you are short of the troops you need.

King making and surrender monkeys can also spoil any satisfaction of a hard earned victory.
  
Guess the above things make quite a strong argument that FFG nailed the tone of GoTs. Thing is this overall feel leaves me feeling depressed and constrained as a result.

Only ever play with the full 6 players, or 5 players if you can use the 1st edition 5 player board. Playing with less vastly over/under powers the remaining colours who are suddenly left with huge areas of extra space to control.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This represents one of the first hybrid designs to mix ameritrash with euro-styled mechanics to create an enagaging experience.

The world of Westeros is used as the backdrop here and up to 6 can play. 6 is still the preferable number of players but some decent rules allow neutral realms to function fairly well in the game. 

The play is governed by needing to control provinces that offer power and strongholds.

Power is used in the blind-bidding auctions, which control turn order, tie-breaks in combat and strategic benefits. 

Strongholds determine how many new units can be mustered in a given region. 

Combat in the game is purely based on total unit strength rather than any randomised dice rolling, but the inclusion of Support rules between players with adjacent provinces introduces an almost Diplomacy level of negotiation and backstabbing.

Whilst the game's length is controlled by a set number of maximum turns (8) a game can still take between 2-4 hours, with a length closer to 4 hours being more likely. 

I really wanted to like this game but in truth i don't dig the nature of combat and the blind bidding. 

For me this game really requires the right mix of players that fully understand the system and can play at a decent clip.

So rating down from 9 to 8 because of this.   

Still an excellent game, albeit a tad too long. I'd recommend it only with 5-6 players.

RATING: 2
Perhaps you need to be a fan of the books to appreciate this game. There is nothing original in the gameplay but rather an ordinary battle game with Game of Thrones pasted on it, with the main difference that the mechanisms seem to have been chosen to fit the theme rather than to add value to the game.

Battles are fought with special ability cards, which means that you need to know the cards to increase your odds of winning. Diplomacy is limited since the players cannot support each other. Player control is limited too since you are dependent on random events to build your kingdom or army. And what's the point of the wildings? Pay to avoid damage for all? Why pay at all?

My first (and only) game had four phases:

1. Grab the empty land
2. Wait, since you couldn't attack your neighbor without expose yourself to your other neighbor
3. Attack in the second to last turn to end up biggest
4. If the attack didn't succeed, take on the kingmaker role and attack the one you don't want to win

If you like diplomacy, play Diplomacy instead, and if you like random battles, play RISK instead.

A) Des amis y ont joué 10 fois (à 3, 4, 5 et 6 joueurs): 10 victoires des Baratheon!; 

B) La "Westeros Phase": Une partie dure plusieurs heures, et généralement, "hasard" et "longues parties" ne s'arriment pas bien ensemble. C'est un peu ce que je reproche de la "Westeros Phase": Une bonne idée peut s'avérer mauvaise, ou vice-versa, selon les cartes d'événements "Westeros" qui seront dévoilées à chaque tour (1).

C) J'aime l'aspect "diplomatique" du jeu, mais ceci peut à la fois allonger terriblement la durée de la partie, et ne s'adapte pas si bien dans des parties avec 3 (ou même 4) joueurs. Comme une partie a un nombre de tours limités, il est difficile de réagir suite à une trahison... Tout le contraire du jeu Diplomacy!


(1) - Première partie jouée (4 joueurs, avec les Stark): Impossible pour moi d'acquérir des Power Tokens. Il est vrai que j'ai peut-être exécuté un mauvais déplacement au Tour 1 (m'éloignant d'une case "Supply"), mais le fait que la carte "Supply" du Westeros Phase ait été dévoilée aux tours *1 et *2 de la partie a fait en sorte que je n'ai jamais pu augmenter mon score à ce niveau. J'ai donc été passif toute la partie dans les votes et l'influence.

Deuxième partie jouée (3 joueurs, avec les Stark): Cette fois, ce sont les cartes "Mustering" (recrutement de troupes) puis "Put to the Sword" (impossibilité d'assigner des ordres de défense), dévoilées au coeur de la partie mais à un bien mauvais moment pour moi, qui ont grandement altéré la suite de la partie...

RATING: 7
I have not watched the television show, and I made it 3/4 of the way through the first book before hurling it away in disgust.  Nonetheless, I really enjoy playing this game. It reminds me of so many games that I played as a teen -- zero-sum, power games with much pleading and ganging up and cursing of bad rolls.  The map is quite beautiful and well-thought out as far as forcing certain types of conflict and movement.  There is the usual down-time when an individual player is taking their turn and moving / resolving battles with some other player.  However, for THAT style of game, this one is fun.

Now, that said, I've only played with 6 players.  Also, I do think that the game suffers from a bit of fiddliness in its rules and more importantly from the fact that a player can get pummeled to the point where they are basically out of the game...and there might still be 2 hours of game to play through!  In fact, this has happened in every game that I've played.  There are 3 or 4 players who are clearly duking it out, 1 or 2 that *might* be able to sneak out a victory, and 1 who has been crushed long before the game is over.  But hey, in this style of game...an area control configuration that requires taking territory from others and repelling their attempts to take territory from you.  It's a game in which luck and player alliances play a role, so I don't see how games won't have that odd-person out.  (Btw, I have no patience for the folks who want to take the "luck" out of the game by eliminating the battle cards.  Suck it up and save your lamentations for other games.  This ain't a Euro!)

However, like I say, all-in-all this game provides a tense, epic all out struggle between kingdoms that have very slight, but very nice, unique styles of play connected with them.  I've been that pummeled player, and during those times the game is less fun.  I've been the almost victor, and during those times the game was exhausting, satisfying, and oddly anti-climactic..mainly because everyone else ends up slightly pissed off.  

Anyway, friends who know the fictional story say that this game captures the narrative quite well.  FFG seems pretty good at that.  Me, I don't know the story, and what I encountered, I did not enjoy.  The game, though, I do.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A bloated Dune-inspired "conquer the map" extravaganza. If it feels like an already complicated game with one too many expansions dumped on top, is because that's pretty much what it is. As this 2nd edition consolidates the previous edition's most notable expansions in the same box.

Thus, you have 2 separate phases of bidding over event and conditions, there are 5 different tracks of conditions to keep your eye on, and combat has 4 separate interlocking mechanics that try to brute-force hidden actions, unit placement, special powers and luck of the draw in a single abominable element.

I love ameritrashers, but GoT is easily one of FFG's most bloated and unbearable games. Its a festival of excess that collapses under its own weight and lacks the ingenuity of the games it tries to emulate.

RATING: 7
A Game of Thrones is a great war game at the 3-player level, but suffers from severe balance issues at 4 & 5 players.

The 4-player's game balance is atrocious. The addition of the Greyjoy faction provides a thorn in the side of both the Stark and Lannister families, but leaves Baratheon untouched. Plus, the 4-player setting opens areas to the south that were previously locked off easily within reach of the Baratheon player.

A 5-player game is still unbalanced. The 5th player plays the Tyrells which add a bit of competition for the Baratheons in the south, but play for the Lannisters is worse than ever as they're the only faction easily able to be attacked by all 4 other families.

Balance is restored on a 6-player game although combat is still limited to a faction's nearest neighbors. Lannisters remain the punching bag they were in the 5-player game, but at least the addition of the Martells draws some of the Tyrell and Baratheon attention.

Recommendations for 4 & 5 player games: 

4) Play Lannister, Baratheon, Tyrell, and Martell and keep all territories above the neck locked out to balance the game out.

5) Keep Pike locked out. Play a Stark, Lannister, Baratheon, Tyrell, Martell game and voila! balance is restored.

aGoT is a long game though that can afflict players with analysis paralysis, so it will likely dominate an entire gaming evening.

RATING: 8.5
There is much to like about this game - great for the nights you have 5-6 people (or 4 if you have the expansion). I especially like that so much of the planning is done simultaneously amongst the players - you do not have to wait around for each player as each person is planning their actions at the same time.  Resolution of the decisions you do make is fairly quick - not a long mechanical process.  The THEME is great, especially for anyone immersed in the books/tv show.  The rules are fairly simple, except ports are a little convoluted.  Combat as is makes for a relatively predictable outcome with few direct casualties - but the optional cards (included) make combat more unpredictable and deadly.

RATING: N/A
規則:
*野人牌堆洗勻放右上，野人標記放2，事件牌根據數字分類洗運放一旁，拿對應人數的中立兵團標記放在圖板對應名字區，回合標記放1，3人時烏鴉權力軌放置額外圖板覆蓋
*玩家選擇一個家族拿所有配件，分數影響力跟補給標記根據家族檔版放在對應位置，少於六人時，影響力標記往左移填補空格，然後三個軌最高的都拿對應指示物，再根據檔版放起始兵力，佔城標記也根據現況記錄在主圖版，權力標記拿五個
*遊戲進行最多10輪，每輪有三個階段，如果有玩家佔領七個有城堡或要塞的區域則直接獲勝，否則遊戲結束佔領最多的獲勝，平手比要塞在比補給在比影響力資源在比鐵王座軌
1.維斯特路階段(第一輪跳過)  回合標記前進一格，每個數字的事件卡都抽一張，根據三張卡上的野人標記推進野人軌，如果起始就在12則結算野人進攻，之後照數字結算三張事件卡
>補給  每個玩家計算至有領土上的補給標記並跟新補給軌，根據目前補給軌顯示，該玩家能有的兵團(一個區域的兵數)數根該兵團的大小，超過的兵直接移除，兩個以上才算是兵團
>徵兵  每個有城堡/要塞有1/2點徵兵值，士兵1點，騎士或攻城車兩點，且可花一點從士兵升級，船1點，只能放在該區域或相鄰海域(沒有敵兵)或港口
>競標(爭王)  所有玩家先展示所有影響力資源，然後用這個來暗標，從鐵王座開始根據出的價格調整順位，平手鐵王座玩家決定，全部標完影響力資源再放回面前 
*鐵王座的順序同時時玩家行動的順位，所有戰爭以外平手都由此玩家決定結果
*劍  戰爭平手比此軌順位高的贏，一輪中可以在一場戰爭武力+1
*皇冠軌 越靠近1能用越多星號指令，第一拿烏鴉指示物，每次計畫階段結束，可選擇一個場上跟手上指令交換或是可以看野人卡頂卡之後可放回或置底
2.計畫階段  玩家先同時放置一個指令蓋著到所有自己有一單位的領土，可嘴砲，之後全部打開，然後有烏鴉標記玩家可啟動能力
>火把  可移除附近一個拳頭/火把/皇冠指令，如果成功移除皇冠則該玩家少一個影響力資源你多一個，陸上的不能移除海上的，反之可以，帶星的可以移除盔甲
>斧頭 該區單位可移動到相鄰(多個可目標不同)，但最多只能觸發一場戰鬥，陸軍可靠海上連結的船隊移動到另外一塊陸地，船可移動到港口
>盔甲 被打時防禦加成
>拳頭 可支援相鄰區的戰爭
>皇冠 獲得1+領土上皇冠數量的影響力資源，海上沒效果，帶星的可以改為對該地徵兵
*指令不夠時當輪則照順位放置指令，有空的不放
3.行動階段  先照鐵王座順位結算火把指令，在重新照順位結算斧頭指令，在結算皇冠指令，使用後指令清空，最後再把防禦跟支援指令清掉，倒下的單位站起
*移動後如果有戰鬥，根據指令值加減，加上單位戰力(步兵1騎兵2攻城車攻城時4(有要塞或城堡)船1)
*可放一個影響力資源(從供應區)在移動後空地上代表佔領，之後有人來則棄掉
*戰爭結算  先求支援，有支援指令的可以提供相鄰戰場同等其兵力的支援，一輪多次，船可支援相鄰陸戰，兩方加總此時戰力總和，然後在一起暗出一張領袖卡結算(用完棄掉，最後一張棄掉時把之前六張拿起可重用)，加總左上戰力(下方有劍代表對方要死一個單位，但要贏才有效，有城牆可防一個劍)之後有劍標記的可+1戰力，輸的一起撤退到一個相鄰地點，進攻方退回原位置，防守方則退到分進攻方來的空地或自己領地，且不能超過補給上限，撤退單位放倒，下次戰鬥不提供戰力也不能成為死亡目標或移動，且在撤退就死掉，如果攻擊方獲勝，移除防守方該地的指令
*撤退時可用船，攻城車不能撤退，直接毀滅
*野人入侵 先確認此時也人數值，所有人暗標影響力資源，加總，高於等於野人戰力時成功，最高標得獲得好處，野人軌退回0，失敗的話野人軌退兩格，開一張野人卡結算，之後該卡放到牌庫底
*港口 擁有該相鄰領地的人擁有，可停船上限3，可徵船到港口即使相鄰海域有兵，港口不能被攻擊，可移動到相鄰海域或從海域移進來，港口單位可支援相鄰海域戰鬥，掠奪只能對海上或被海上掠奪，皇冠指令如果相鄰海域有人則取消無效果，且特殊皇冠不能用來在此徵兵，如果佔領敵方港口有船的城市，則把船換成你的(不夠的移除)，權力遊戲事件時，如果相鄰海域沒敵船且港口有一艘可收一影響力資源
*中立軍 不回被敵方支援，不能出卡，要能贏才能進去，劍也不能使用，戰力相等即可，~符號代表不能進入
*堡壘標記 放在主家園裡，戰力加二的單位，打敗移除，單獨時不能被分配指令，且不能支援相鄰地點，也不被其他影響單位效果影響
*河流會阻斷相鄰，遊戲過程中如果調整影響力軌，會根據新的位置分配標記
*指令標記隱藏，影響力資源在競標前要公布總數
****少於六人時***
*5人不使用Martell，起始9個中立
*4人不使用Martell跟Tyrell，起始12個中立
*3人不使用上面兩個跟Greyjoy，起始14個中立，跟使用替代的皇冠軌
***戰爭浪潮模組
*起始洗勻對應卡牌 戰鬥時，出完領袖卡後每人抽一張，如果有劍標記的可使用標記棄掉重抽一張，然後在一起打開，骷顱標記代表對手一定會死掉一個單位
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Having some of the original expansion stuff included in the second edition makes it an automatic improvement over first edition, but the gameplay experience doesn't change that much, so the first edition comment remains, being:
The pace feels about right for what it was. I was thinking all the way through, hmmm:
- movement, units and support rules from Diplomacy
- add resources to supply centres a la Borderlands (& Mare Nostrum & Wallenstein)
- steal the pre-ordering of defined functions from Wallenstein (& crowns=wheat)
- pinch the combat cards from LotR: Confrontation

et voila, some pretty good heritage.

I was expecting more real alliance Diplomacy however, but really it's like any other multi-player wargame where you agree shared boundaries, and pick your time for breaking them to shoot for the win. We don't see real joint alliance play where two players combine to wipe someone out, then move on to the next objective. Maybe that's just our group think though. Too nicey nice.

But if you want to play a bash up the leader multi-player wargame, this played easily and enjoyably. But the experience for me wasn't much different from Mare Nostrum or any of the others. Because it's multi-player bashup, it'd probably be an improvement if the game ended randomly, with increasing chances each turn, to encourage everyone to go for it each turn without the waiting, waiting, waiting that the game can produce, where there's little change in state for an hour or so in the middle-game.

RATING: 9.5
The main mechanic is that you place orders on each area for which you have troops and then use those troops to either move (and possibly battle), support others, gain influence tokens or defend. But there is so much that goes with that. The play is asymmetric so each house has its own strengths and weaknesses due to their location and battle cards as well as their starting influence on the three influence tracks. Each game is unique and the interaction between players is significant. This is a must try regardless of whether you like the books/show.

RATING: 9
Probably my favourite game, but only with 6 players (though you can player lower player counts with the Feast for Crows expansion). It's Dipomacy meets eurogaming done right.

This game has the simulaneous orders aspect of Diplomacy that I love, without all the pain of written and incorrect orders.

It has bidding.

It has combat, also diplomacy-style with support, convoys and all that.

It has bluffing, hand management, resource managment.

It has high levels of interaction.

Asymmetric powers.

Great components, gorgeous board.

Does everybody like it though? Nope. You better have a thick skin. A weak player can unintentionally throw the game to their neighbour. It can take a while, I've always finished in a normal game night, but I've heard of 8phour games that don't complete??? The games I've played there hasn't been a lot of diplomacy going on, maybe why it finishes in reasonable time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I like how well this game flows, especially for a six-player title. Rulebook is rather long, but after a game or two everything just clicks and one gets to appreciate the elegant design. AGOT is very conflict driven as neighboring players connect their borders very early from the start.

Components are very nice, but I'd prefer wooden units instead of faux-marble plastic ones. 

Deterministic battles take some thrill from the combat because most of the time the outcome is known the moment a region is attacked. It is easy to follow which nobles the opposing player has left and account for this.

Unfortunately, if players are not very competitive and avoid conflict, game will be bland and boring. Plotting, shifting alliances and table talk are at heart of this title.

RATING: N/A
The rulebook requires you read through it in entirety before playing a game. We just wanted to play the game, so decided to read it as we went along - as we do with most boardgames. A lot of what was written in the rulebook was fairly badly worded (or could have been done a lot simpler), and even after reading I'm not entirely sure of the point of ports. 

The rulebook wasn't very obviously laid out either, meaning many references to the index were required. 

Having read and condensed the rules, we are going to try and play it again later, but as we only have three players (at the moment), it might not be as fun as with more. I can certainly see a lot of room for discussion and planning with other players.

RATING: 8
Game of strategy and diplomacy with lots of backstabbing :)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The second edition brings with it lots of subtle re-balancing, which helps the game immensely. It's a tight 'dudes on a map' game where your opponents are at your borders looking for signs of weakness. Expand too far, and your home becomes vulnerable, but don't expand at all, and risk not making any progress. It's a game where you absolutely need to ally with one neighbor and fight the other, but keep your eyes open always for signs of treachery.

For the second edition they removed these castle pieces that protected strongholds from horses, but they kept in catapults that are special units used to attack only strongholds. I thought that was a strange choice. I'm also never sure how I feel about Tides of War cards which add a random element to an otherwise calculated combat system.

A lot of new, more compelling 'dudes on the map' games have come out in the last few years. Although thinking about this game makes me sentimental, ultimately this game always winds up a turtling contest until the last turn when everyone makes their move. Sure, there's usually a little jockeying back and forth for a spot here or there, but the map creates natural defensible borders, and if players start conflicts too early, they always see their neighbor on the other side move in. I suppose that's thematic, but the game is too long an experience for something that really boils down to 2 important turns right at the end.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I love the limited resource combat style of this game, which requires the same sort of alliances and deals as Diplomacy.  Along with that, the orders mechanic is such a great hidden information game that's going on there.  I love that you try to do as much as possible within the confines of the track you're on.

The tracks themselves are very interesting as well and add lots of theme and some fun stuff to use those influence tokens you acquire on.

This version of the game significantly improved the Westeros deck, which was my big complaint from the first edition.  I love the added flexibility that it gives to the leader on a given track and the allowance that you have more opportunities to get the phases you need.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A Game of Thrones: The Board Game is a great game, and one of my favorites for larger groups. My gaming group always enjoys playing and I rarely have trouble finding six people willing to play, although it is also quite fun with less than six players. The game does a decent job of making it fair with any amount of players, but certain areas can be exploited more easily when you have less than five or six.  There is very little luck involved in the game, which I enjoy. Combat is done through the power of the pieces involved on the board, move orders placed by players at the beginning of each turn and combat cards which you choose during combat and reveal simultaneously. Combat being done this way is a lot more exciting than just rolling dice or having some other random determinate. There are many different strategies involving the different houses (factions) and working together or against other players to take over the most castles. 

The only problem with the six player game from my perspective is slight inequality in the different houses starting set ups. While I've seen every individual house win at some point in my play throughs, The Baratheons have a very powerful and open starting location while some houses like the Greyjoys and the Lannisters have far less room to expand before they will have to pick fights with other players. 

Overall though, it is a very entertaining game with great replay value and I recommend it for anyone who enjoys military based strategy games.

RATING: 5
Now this game has serious design flaws. It's carried by theme alone - you will play it twice and realize something's wrong, you will probably think it's balancing issues and never touch it again but have fond memories of the first 2 plays. This game is definitely not worth its price, it's just a mess of barely connected mechanics. The game doesn't know if it wants to be Diplomacy's stream-lined modern successor or a quick random strategy game for fun so it ends up being very disappointing and pointless. You will have fun on your first play when you get to play as Starks and make alliances and backstabs and plans but you will quickly notice this should be more enjoyable. As soon as you start thinking about the game and what other players are doing the spell is gone and whatever fun you had impersonating characters is gone. If you want a game that's only held together by theme go play cops and robbers outside!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
NOTE: This game is likely going to get an update to a '10'. I want to play the four player variant before I make my decision on this score. I've played this game three times now and every single time I walk away entirely pleased with the experience, reviewing what went right and wrong in my head, and how to do a better job next time. And, most importantly, wanting to play again as soon as possible.

My love of this game has almost nothing to do with the theme and almost everything to do with the mechanics. Diplomatic interactions, asymmetric powers, hidden orders, secret bidding, random events, and a touch of cooperative (but not too much!). There are so many great mechanics going on this game and they work so well together. The phases of game play are rich and diverse layers. You don't have to be a fan of the books (or the TV series) to appreciate this game. This game could have been set in any age or realm and still worked well.

I love this game, but it's a harder one to get to the table due to the length.

RATING: 9
I love the secret orders and deterministic combat. You definitely need something of a poker face, you can outmaneuver your opponents just by looking at what part of the board they are looking at. The game length is long, but never feels boring. The theme is excellently integrated and we love to make quotes from the show like "a Lannister always pays his debts". The components are of excellent quality.

My only criticism is some of the rules are "picky" and hard to nail down until you've played two or three times. things like siege engines can't retreat, you can march out of a port but not into one, and you can't consolidate power in a sea area.

RATING: 10
An outstanding board game with elegant design and rules that requires deep thinking. Its complexity is one of the reasons why I love it so much. It can be frustrating to learn for new players. Still, I wouldn't hesitate teaching and miss playing this game. With some good friends, this game becomes amazingly fun.

I have never played a full 6-player game, but I did play from 3 to 4 which have been tons of fun. When you play such games, you have to set a rule for alliances to prevent players from teaming up on just one player. This is especially true in 3-player games. Also, you have to make some areas impassable to make certain houses come and fight everyone.

Edit: After 10+ games, it is still engaging and fun. I can't wait to get my hands on the Feast for Crows expansion though.

RATING: 9
Clever and enjoyable mechanics everywhere, excellent production. Truly an amazing way to pit strategic, ruthless friends against each other in a thoroughly tense game.

Objective flaw: Takes too long (about 1h per player, with a floor of 3h, in my experience). Unless you've got dedicated friends, it will be hard to get going.

Subjective flaw: Due to the length, players get invested. And some people cannot handle the backstabbing. Can sour a night, even cause friendship tension.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I love the show, so was obviously interested to have a try of the board game having heard a few good reviews.

It does have some good mechanics. Area control and resource management are key concepts. As everyone has borders with other players, it is very important to maintain good relations with your immediate neighbours early. But the very nature of this game is deceit, and you will have to attack sooner, rather than later. It's just a case of who betrays first...

Overall, it's pretty decent and I'm not adverse to commiting the time to play. Does need a decent group of players to play, though. If you get the case of someone "just making up the numbers", they make it very easy on their neighbours. This does unbalance things considerably.

Definitely worth trying out!

(-) It takes a long time and big dedication to explain the game to others, mainly due to some exeptions (ports, ships, special rules regarding support and raid used on ports or sea areas, ...)

Playtime: 40 - 60 min / player

RATING: 10
I had my reservations prior to buying this game after hearing about people's opinions on how the game is unbalanced. Have to say that personally I do not think this is true. While yes, you do have to play towards your strengths as a house, by no means is one house more in favor of winning than the others. 

The only draw back is that you need six people to get the full experience. If you don't, the game becomes about a 8 or 9 just because the way the houses are placed and the weakness of neutral territories. But overall this game is KILLER. If you do not like confrontation to not get this game. But if you like making deals then stabbing your opponent in the back through bidding and subterfuge there is not one I have play that has been better. For all the people whining about this aspect of the game don't play it and go buy a Euro city building game or something.

RATING: 6
The map's not as nice or easy one the eyes. And the old raven's a bit cuter too. The wood components probably preferable to the plastic. 

The rulebook is clear (altho' ports seem tricky)!

In some ways it is strongly themed, in others not so much, as the designers went the route of making the powers pretty symmetrical, apart from their obvious geographic differences and the special powers in the House cards. A game like Sword of Rome took a different route, making the powers more distinct. Which is better from the "realities" of the books, I don't know.

My first play was with five and it worked well, although I suspect Tyrell may have had a bit easier without an active Martell around. Should shine with six as a pretty brilliant, interactive game. I came in second with Stark, coming from behind after finally scraping together just enough resources.  

Long game, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Second edition fixes some flaws in the first edition and introduces some elements of the original first expansion, but is not without problems either. The ports are here, all right, as well as house Martell. The siege engines also made it, but curiously not the fortifications, so it's now much easier to storm a castle than to defend it, which is non-thematic, unfortunate and detracts from strategy in the game. New house cards are again unbalanced, with some (Balon Greyjoy) being definitely too powerful. Overall, a very good game which could easily be almost perfect if not for (I presume) hasty publishing process of FFG.

The one flaw is that, without randomizer in the form of Tides of Battles cards (which increase or reduce your battle strength randomly), my games never end prematurely - conquering 7 castles is next to impossible with competent opposition.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
 This is a very involving game, absolutely minimal down time, and from the second turn the tree of possible moves and interactions pretty much explodes, so that it seems unlikely that any two games will be the same, and players must keep their wits about them. The mechanics seem appropriate to the theme, even though the euro-ish nature of many mechanics (the various checks and balances, and of course luckless combat) will not appeal to everybody - they should appeal to plenty, though, and for my taste really make the game, as its fascinating how uncertain things can be in spite of the lack of dice. This is by no means a unique feature to this game, but here (as compared say to War of the Roses Lancaster vs York) it somehow manages to achieve the Ameritrash feel. 

I own but have not yet played 1st edition, so can't really comment on the play differences in editions, but would say that graphically the components of this edition are much more pleasing, and though I would prefer them in wood, the plastics pawns are nice enough.

RATING: 7
Only played it once. Only bought the 2nd edition 'cause I'm a sucker! All my soul are belong to GRRM. To clarify: I don't dislike this game. It doesn't get played, that's all. Probably could have been satisfied with owning the first edition. Who am I kidding? Lol.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like the clearer map and the whole shebang in the one box. The rulebook is easier to find what one needs to find than in the first edition. I greatly prefer the wooden pieces of the first edition to the new cheap feeling plastic pieces. A bit sad that the one use tiles didn't make it into this version, but there's more than just the basics in the one box and if that's all the shelf room you've got to invest in A Game of Thrones then this is the edition to get. It's a mix of the original and the best tweaks from the first edition expansions.

Five rules to remember:
1. Special Consolidate Power Order Token can be used to initiate mustering in its area.
2. Only one combat is allowed per march order.
3. The Messenger Raven token allows the holder to also look at the top card of the Wildling deck and place the card at the bottom of the deck if she chooses.
4. Ports with a friendly unit receive a power token (when the GoT Westeros card is resolved), as long as the adjacent sea is not occupied with enemy ships (i.e. blockaded).
5. When you raid a Consolidate Power order not only do you receive a power token, but the player losing the Consolidate Power order has to give up one of their power tokens.

The game runs a little long for my taste. You could probably get most of the experience in six turns. After a few turns the game seems to become a waiting game anyway - position yourself for the final push, then go for it. A few ideas have been suggested to speed things along - something like rolling a die at the beginning of each turn after turn five and on a 4-6 the game will end at the end of that turn. I'd like to try it because I'm going to like the game more if it's a two hour experience and not a four hour, ten turn experience. On the other hand it might reduce the likelihood of a player getting a seven castle win and the kind of swings you can get in the game that make it kind of fun.

Alliances seem to be tricky to form in our group. Apparently the temptation for small raiding gains is not conducive to multi-turn mutual support for mutual beneficial position. Sure, ultimately there's the inevitable and predictable betrayal, but usually in alliance games you'd better make it count when you do decide to backstab. With our constant backstabbing and resulting trust issues the furthest we ever seem to get is... well, not very far. Death by a thousand cuts really.

RATING: 7
Main thoughts are already posted with the old edition. As for this one, art is gorgeous, especially on the cards, but can get too busy and bordering on hard-to-see on the board (red borders for sea regions? wtf?). Marbled plastic minis, instead of wood, add to the busy-ness. [-]Tides of Battle is an utter fail; I get that it's the successor to the Clash of Kings variant, but it could have been less swing-y.[/-] I appreciate that the best parts of the expansions are here (e.g. Ports, Westeros Cards that give benefits to title-holders). Not sure I like Siege Engines though, especially since there are no Forts.

6er is where it's at (though Martell and Lannister seem squeezed). 4-5 are not playable out of the box and need house-rules because the neutral tokens can't stand against siege engines. 3er is okay, just takes a while to get the battles going, and there's little diplomacy, just straight-up outguessing.

A little shocking that the designers actually thought 4- and 5-player were balanced after including siege engines (just because the players balance the game doesn't mean you get to throw out a woefully unbalanced thing to start with), especially since they got it mostly right in 1ed. But no big deal, use my simple fixes.

[u]selwyth's ultimate game-balancers[/u]
6er: 14 non-capital cities for 6 players, 2.3/player.
5er: Set it up as usual, but then flip Sunspear and Yronwood over to make them impassable. 13 castles for 5, 2.6/player. Starfall/Storm's End is okay due to Tyrell vs. Baratheon. Or alternatively, flip them all over except Sunspear.
4er: Additionally, make Highgarden, Oldtown, Storm's End and Starfall impassable (the 3er board). 10 for 4, 2.5/player.
3er: Play something else. If you really must, make Supply or Power the first tiebreaker instead to curb Lannister's stronghold advantage.

[hr]
Lowered design rating. Shocking imbalance with 4-5er. Lannister-Greyjoy is too swing-y. And all-round lack of thematic integration (circa early 2000s FFG) that fails to capitalize on the awesome theme; it's disappointing they didn't re-design from the ground up, or re-skin a design with more treachery and intrigue, like DUNE. This is the biggest letdown for me. Also, the rich gets richer in this game (and it's very hard to prop up the weaklings), and it sucks to be caught in no-man's-land without any chance at victory (and you can sometimes identify this pretty early).

Also, this is poster child for 'unnecessarily 6-player'. There are 2 players you're neighbors with, and the others can be virtually invisible to you, with opportunities for cooperation coming up rarely, resulting in unnecessary downtime. 4-player works much better, as seen in A Storm of Swords and A Feast for Crows expansions.

Also, one cannot anoint this as a 'negotiation game' or emblematic of the intrigue in the source material when [i]you can't even trade your power tokens[/i]! Seriously considering that as a house rule.

3+4

RATING: 7
[i]Overrated, flawed, and outclassed -- but kinda good anyway.[/i]

solid game, kept from greatness by some problems:

1) Big crowded map full of players you will never be able to reach. Seriously. Optimistically, you will have 6 players for this -- half of them will be increasing your game length by 2-3 hours, and you can't really interact with them.

2) Rich get richer element -- because of the crowded map, for some players to be moving ahead towards the victory condition, others have to be putting permanently behind. (with limited ways to impact the winners (most often they just kingmake). The spots that push you towards victory ALSO contain resources and mustering points. *However, I now think this can be mitigated SLIGHTLY with great players, as they will not only not allow someone to get too far ahead, but also not allow their neighbors to get too far behind.

3) Kingmaking -- this game is all about manuevering into position where you are one of the contenders for a win on the last turn, since with good players no one is winning early. Whether you win or not on the last turn just depends on you making zero mistakes and hoping the rest of the battles between the kingmakers and your competition go your way-- it's largely out of your hands. Yes, all your hard work comes down to this!

4) Combat and it's effect on the game -- the combat system, in a vacuum, is OK -- deterministic, yet it _sometimes_ rewards mind games. If they know you have a guaranteed win in your hand, maybe you don't play it because they're going to sandbag? But then what if they know you want to save your best card? Etc. It's usually just a mixup between 2 options or so, and it works OK. But the deterministic combat has an effect on the game -- people usually won't attack the other player unless they know they can win, or if the battle is worth it to them for some other reason. This isn't inherently a problem, but leads to turtley game and predictability. That leads me to the next thing...

5) (Optional) Tide of Battle Cards -- These cards make the deterministic combat not really deterministic. When I heard the concept, I thought maybe they'd help break up the turtley conditions and stalemates caused by deterministic combat where you only attack if you KNOW you win/benefit. Maybe Tide of Battle cards reward risk taking occasionally? Nope. They are not to be counted on, and actually increase the amount of unit death in the game, which ruin peoples plans and FURTHER constrain you in a game where unit constraint is already a big deal. And the worst part is that Tides of Battle cards don't seem to actually change the way players play  -- instead, it just randomizes the results they get. Luck in games is fine, even long ones like this, but IMO luck should impact your decisions -- here I don't think it does.

6) Setting -- The game has the Game of Thrones license, set in Westeros, and populated by a couple dozen iconic characters... except not really. As my friend once said after trying this for the first time, "This game could have just as easily been named Soldiers, Knights, & Boats" and it wouldn't be a lesser game for it. The Game of Thrones setting, while fantastic, is basically squandered

7) Negotiation/Backstabbing -- Yes, it exists, kind of? Without hidden info or ways for players to actually exhcnage anything (currency, positions, etc), this seems largely based on just analyzing who's in the lead and making informed decisions. OR taking advantage of naive players who are not good at doing so. Yeah, you'll be "backstabbed" after making a deal, but you KNOW you will because only one player can win (so it's like any other game), and you usually know when, how, and why. IMO. Maybe I'm missing some stuff though here, not sure about this one.

8) Competition/Length -- I love long games.Don't have the problem with the length. But the length on this one, and its player count, puts it in direct competition with Dune, the other long, epic 6 player game about alliances and betrayals. But.... Dune doesn't suffer from ANY of the problems above! Dune opens up its map and interaction, letting you battle and trade with whomever you please. Instead of being held back by dividing players into run-away potential winners and kingmakers (losers), it lets the winners officially ally against the losers to close the game out early and not have to worry about such things. Combat in Dune tends towards deterministic, but mind games are ALWAYS present, so the result is a battle that feels strategic yet unpredictable. Dune makes fantastic use of its setting, and backstabbing is an option rather than a certainty so when you are betrayed it stings all the more!

All of this may make it seem like I hate this game, but I really don't. I've never been completely bored playing it or anything. It just comes so close to something I could fully embrace but falls short which is why I'm hard on it.

[b]I'd Rather Play:[/b] Dune, Rex: Final Days of an Empire

(4 Design + 3 Enjoyment)
