RATING: 6
A decent game with some nice thematic touches. Unfortunately, it suffers from a lot of the problems common to games like this -- kingmaking, randomness that makes for bad experiences (we played a game in which ALL musters came out before ANY supplies, and ALL Game of Thrones came out before ANY Clash of Kings -- it was awful), and a general overstaying of its welcome. Either someone blunders, or the game bogs down to a repetitive back-and forth after a certain number of turns. A player whose neighbors play poorly has a strong advantage. I would not turn down an offer to play, but I probably would not suggest it, either.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This could very well become the best and favorite game of your gaming group... but only if two things happen:

1. If you have the full 5-6 players, you're going to have a good game.  If you have 4 or less... it's not fun... in fact, it's pretty boring and virtually impossible to play the main campaign (by "main campaign", I'm thinking about the contrast between the main story and the small-scale scenarios that the first edition expansion sets came with).

2. If no one is broken/defeated after one or two turns.  It can be easy for new players who aren't paying attention to completely wreck the Greyjoys and Lannisters, and essentially make it impossible for one side or the other to win.  That's hugely frustrating for new players, and can turn into a deeply unsatisfying game.

But otherwise, this is a beautiful game.  Not only is it physically beautiful from the board to the cards, but the entire concept of hidden orders that are revealed at the same time offers tons of replay and strategic value.  No dice!  Hidden character cards that can win a battle!  Chaining march orders to make a sneak attack!  Raids that can cancel an opponent's support order, which will then make your main attack succeed!  This is a real strategy game.

The set-up isn't that extensive (okay, the first time or two will take 30-45 minutes to set up and get the rules, but after the first two games, summarizing the rules and setting up for a game can take as little as 10-15 minutes).   I quite like complex games, but you don't have to in order to get this game.

Super fun strategy game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm not an expert on war/diplomacy games but A Game of Trones The Board Game is one of the best games i have ever played. It's long, it's prone to AP and it can be unbalanced if you play with less than 6 players (there are more balanced variants on the forum for 4-5 players though), but it is nonetheless, a fantastic game imo.

The simultaneous action selection and no-dice-combat works like a charm. There is luck involved (random cards in the Westeros phase and Wildling attack), but it adds to the game in a good way. It's tense from the very beginning and the theme is great. I love it.

RATING: 8.5
Very good conquest-style game. I like how each of the houses has slightly different abilities. The management of supply is also a clever mechanic that reduces the size of armies and prevents anyone from amassing too much force. In addition, I think that the use of influence tokens to change player order and tiebreakers is pretty cool. Combine that with the limitations on how many starred tokens you can play and simultaneous action selection with putting down your orders, the game actually moves pretty quickly. There are issues with player count with less than the full complement of 6, unfortunately.

RATING: 9
The game itself is excellent... I haven't decided if it's superior to the 1st edition, but it may very well be.  My only complaint is the siege engines -- really can't stand them in play.  Too swingy.  I feel the same way regarding the alternative leaders, but as they're clearly "optional" I've no problem with their inclusion.

The biggest complaint is that the board itself isn't as easily read (though it's certainly beautiful), and the marbled plastic bits aren't nearly as nice as the old wooden ones.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is an alright game but not one that is particularly for me.  It is a combat oriented area control game and it feels very much like a war game but I'm sure war game fans would take issue with that.

The last couple of times I played a war game with any kind of weight were Axis and Allies: 1942 and Risk Legacy and I did not enjoy them at all.  This game shares a lot of the problems I have with those games.  I'm not a fan of static setups for one.  Aside from the amount of time it takes to set up a game there are some balance issues there I think.  It appears that the players in the center of the map are at a disadvantage needing to fight a 2-front war while the Starks and Martells only need to worry about bolstering to their south or north respectively.  This also can cause the game to feel stale if you end up playing the same House multiple times since you always start out the same way and probably need to follow similar strategies.

The combat system is almost identical to Kemet, which I actually like.  In fact, I like this system a little bit better because each House has a deck of combat cards that are unique and many of them have special abilities on them besides the normal combat, casualty, and defense numbers.  I'm a fan of asynchronous player abilities and this works well to differentiate the different Houses, plus if you're a Song of Fire and Ice fan it is neat to be able to see all the different familiar character art.

The art is very good too, in fact the production values of this game are very good.  The pieces are all attractive although they could have been differentiated a little bit more.  Once the board is filled with plastic it can be hard to tell which pieces are footmen, knights, and siege engines at different angles.  The bonus tokens for the influence tracks are gigantic things making them seem to carry a particular weight of importance.  The board is also nice looking and despite the amount of information each area conveys does a good job of not looking cluttered or confusing.  The one other complaint I would levy against the components, and it is minor, is the order tokens should have symbolized which house they belong to on both sides.  In a game with a large number of players and especially with players moving and invading different areas they can get a little mixed up or confusing.

I guess where the game eventually falls for me, and why it is not a keeper, is it doesn't do much to justify the length and trouble it takes to get it played.  Users here on BGG say it is best with 6, which I agree with, and it also takes 3 hours.  I think that's an optimistic estimate with 6 players.  The first time I played with 6, including a rules explanation to be fair, we hit 3 hours at almost the exact halfway mark.  This is a 5-hour game with 6 players, you might be able to trim that to 3.5-4 hours with experienced players but you have to play a few 5 hour games to get players to that point first.  Not to mention getting 6 people together to play a game that long is not an easy task.

And honestly, I just have better (in my opinion) games that fill the same roles.  Kemet is incredibly similar and plays in a 3rd of the time or less and has more variability with the special powers, which is more my style anyway.  Even Small World, also with variable powers, scratches that combat/area control itch.  And if I am going to play a game for 5 hours there are games I would prefer to pull out as well, like Merchant of Venus.  So ultimately I just don't think I would ever reach for this game over others.

RATING: 4
At first I loved this game. I like GoT, I like strategy, I like scheming. BUT... after few games you realise how every game is basicaly the same, at start everybody run to get as much stuff for free as they can and then for majority of the game you end up fighting for 1-2 zones and near the end you start doing some risky manouvers. To that add time you spend playing it, which is almost the whole day and you start avoiding this game. I recently tried a handmade 10 player version with some additional improving rules, but still I felt bit bored. Not cause the player amount but because I kinda realised what's wrong with this game. The mechanics are kinda ok, but the map si the main reason why this game doesnt work. It's narrow and certain player dont meet for the entire game. Unless by some miracle you get control of the sea, there is a very little chance that Martel is gonna meet Stark on the battlefield. If Greyjoy is getting beaten by Stark he has basically no one to turn for help since Greyjoy is basically the only one who can attack Starks. (Yeah Baratheon could try launch something through sea but that would require a lot of effort, that could lose him the game on the other front.) Also the map could you some Landscape combat modifiers, like bonus to defense when the region is attacked from a region across the river, +1 bonus for support as a surprise attack for armies in forest and so on  to make it more stragetic for placing your armies and not just basic straight forward huge army from one region. The scheming part is highly dependant on the players and usually is non-existent. I'm probably rating this game higher than I should.

RATING: 6
I loved this at first, until I realized that it's actually too thematic. Every single game I've played has ultimately been decided by a kingmaker. There's a lot of fiddliness and luck with the order of supply and mustering, and even more if you use the dreaded Tides of Battle cards. It only works with exactly 6 players, and it takes a whole day to play, so it's only a satisfying experience if you enjoy stabbing your friends in the back. And if you like that, more mechanically solid alternatives have come along since this one that require much less time investment.

RATING: 4
For a big box Fantasy Flight game with a high profile license, this game is surprisingly tough and non-random. In fact, most of the variability comes from interaction between the players, scheming and backstabbing. Perfect adaptation of the source material in that sense! However, the game is very complex with a lot of moving parts and small rules to remember. A misunderstanding or a mistake can be really costly for a player. Also there is a strong risk of player elimination, or at least, a player having a really hard time. These factors, plus the long play time, are turning me off. Traded away my unplayed copy for Kemet in June 2014. Very likely to not play this again due to the high player count, long play time and complex rules.

RATING: 8
I'm not sure how I feel about the new edition.  On one hand it seems to have made some much needed simple changes to correct the unbalancing of the houses in the original (especially when playing with less than 5 players).   I feel like this should be a good thing, but now when I play the game it almost feels too balanced.  All the games that I've played always go 10 rounds with not much changing on the board.  For all the short comings of the earlier edition I miss the sudden unexpected victories that seemed more common when playing it. I've played the original to death so it could be that I'm tired of this game and that the small (however meaningful) changes just are not enough to reignite my interest.  

I do enjoy the ADWD expansion though.

RATING: 3
4 hours of dudes on a map, even when played fairly quickly, is too long. Players that are doing badly can conceivably come back in to it, but that's fairly unlikely so a bad turn or two and you're looking at 2+ hours of watching other people think while you're reduced to the role of observer. Also, there really is nothing to prevent people from continuously attacking a weaker player, since each territory has some value, so why not just keep beating up on the weak?

The bidding for the three roles is moderately interesting, but with the amount of randomness in the events and when/how they trigger it's hard to see how the luck of the cards doesn't massively impact who wins. Oh, that and massive kingmaking opportunities.

This recreates and updates a lot of what Diplomacy does. I prefer GoT for being a lot faster, but it still doesn't deliver what I'm looking for in a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game has potential, but it's a slog. One player pushes, the others push him back. Do that for 3-5 hours. Long story short, the game is boring, which makes me sad because, if you've read or watched the series, you want to have fun with this game. And there are bursts of fun, but not enough.

There are limitations on orders, army size, and fighting capabilities. There are frustrating limits on the number of each type of troop you can deploy (why can't I raise another knight? -- just because, that's why). There are even limits on controlling territories you vacate. On the one hand, these restrictions encourage alliances with other players (good). On the other hand, they guarantee you won't hold on to any territory for very long (bad). Any feeling of accomplishment is quickly curtailed and undone unless the other players somehow manage to forget about you (unlikely).

Repeat this back and forth tug of war for up to ten total rounds. Mix in some random events, power cards, and secret auctions that will probably make the game swingy and frustrating. Maybe someone scores an instant victory. If not, everyone makes a wild push during the tenth and final round for whatever they can grab, regardless of their ability to support those final positions. Then someone wins. Did they earn that win? I don't know. More likely, it just so happens they came out ahead in the tug of war for that particular round. If the game was slightly shorter (or longer), someone else would have won -- and I'm not sure it would have that much to do with skill. It feels like it should be skill, but, well, maybe it's not. And that makes the whole experience feel a bit empty.

But the components are pretty, so that's good.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is tough, mean and not forgiving to those who aren't self seeking. Very true to its source material.

I really like the diplomacy that eventuates from the game's need for temporary alliances. As everyone is jostling to keep one another from reaching a game ending position, it's very bad gameplay not to continually forge and fracture alliances. But we are human, and this is where it gets interesting - can I let the Greyjoys get away with taking two of my territories last turn? Should I simply tell them I will support them and then watch them die a horrible death against the Lannisters? Or should I get over it and play 'the Game of Thrones?'

I like that it isn't 'total conquest' but rather relies on an endgame trigger, however I'm not sure how I feel about that combined with a round limit. Maybe it would have been better to let the game be open ended? I like the combat, the ability to support (although doesn't seem to get played enough), and the way actions are selected. The bidding is tense, and external threats make an interesting mix of co-operative and cutthroat competitive play.

RATING: 9.5
This was the first modern boardgame I purchased, and it took more than two years to make it to the table. It was worth the wait!

The game is designed in a manner that you are constantly engaged. Each part of the resolve action phase, raiding, marching units, consolidating power, happens in turn order so you're constantly doing an action or planning your next.

The game balances itself through use of alliances, as a runaway leader will soon be ganged up on. Combat has little random element to it, but still feels unpredictable.

Lastly, the graphic design and component quality it top notch, even for an older FFG game. I love ASOIF, and this is the only war game (besides Memoir '44) I'll ever need)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game is the embodiment of unfair. One player will start with the iron throne and when there is a bid war, and during the bid war, a tie occurs, the person already on the iron throne keeps it. The only way to get someone off the throne is to bid all your influence, which will leave you most likely as the last person on the other tracks due to the player previously on the iron throne still resolving ties.

The pieces in the game are also impossible to move around since they only move one space per turn. There are 10 turns in total and so that means you can only move 10 spaces max in the game. However an attack also counts as a move so if you fail an attack, it was a whole turn wasted going nowhere, making a new enemy and probably leaving yourself vulnerable to attacks from elsewhere on the board.

I understand this is the game of thrones and when you play you win or you die, but oh my god is it hard to die. The way you kill a piece is to use your cards (which the others can ask to see by the way) and use a sword card. The opponent can then simply block with a castle card that they have, leaving you one less powerful card in your hand. What will happen instead is you will push them back into an island by themselves where they will be screwed for the rest of the game. You're then left with a player that sits around watching others think for 6 hours complaining about how they can't do anything, and subsequently making the game even less enjoyable. At least if you die quickly you can do something else, but forcing a player to stay in a game where they have no hope of succeeding is unfair punishment.

I have many other qualms about the game such as bullshit boat victories and the kegs for armies but I think I have said enough. The only thing keeping this game from being lower is that I haven't yet played this game with 6. Hopefully it's a more enjoyable experience (although it's going to need a freakin miracle).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
(+1 play of the first version)
Excellent and thematic diplomacy wargame. From a euro prespective, it's area control at its best.

It has a strong, well intergrated theme, provides strong varying narrative, has varying factions and no luck. You need to outplay, outsmart and deceive your opponents while at the same time negotiating. Multi-track competition and occasional cooperation blend extraordinarilly well together and elevate the game into a masterpiece.

The factions have standard starting positions, and therefore the first turns can perhaps be easily scripted and some alliances appear more often than not. This detracts from replayability but adds to the theme, so for me it is acceptable. Still, it does detract from the diplomatic options quite a bit.

4p is working flawlesly, 5p is even stronger but I expect the 6p games to be even more action packed and fun.

Note: The game has an excellent variant included that is often neglected - the battle cards that affect the results of battles. What these cards practically do is add some minor luck to the battles - not much, just as much as needed. What this means in practice is that players will stop trying to "chess" the game (aka trying to figure all the luck free possibilities to choose from) and be more open to generally attacking, even at a bit suboptimal odds. This greatly speeds the game with many players, and forces a meta game of nerves where picking a general is no longer just a matter of maths, but also a matter of battle importance - as it should be.

RATING: 1
Urgh! This is my absolutely most hated game! I detest every little piece of it. 

Where to even begin? Ok let's start simple: 

Scales absolutely terribly! Nothing but a full game is completely broken! Here's the thing, if you do not have a full game, then some passive, useless, dumb-looking NPCs are placed in replacement. These are always placed at the bottom of the map! Meaning that the player near these have an incredible advantage! As these useless pieces have NO attack capabilities, and there is this unit in the game which is massively strong on the offense, but weak in defense - you can kinda see how this does not work one tiny bit

You have to house rule this otherwise the game is just over before it even begins. (Doesn't help the faction at the top of the map who is just bloody screwed no matter what house rule because they have less movement options and have to spilt their navy into two sides unlike any other faction)

And the randomness, Ohhhh the randomness. You see, you bid randomly like an idiot for the different powers of the game. These bids show up randomly during the game, so sometimes it was good you used all your money to get the most power, and other time the game goes "lolz - we vote again" in just one measly round. Congrats you just lost everything. 

One time I wanted to think long term, so I wanted to get even more income in the 2. round by sacrificing some in the first. But then the game went "LOL screw you" and had us adjust these things only in the first round. (Cause of course this is decided randomly too). Of course there was no adjustment in forever after this, so I just sit around forever with no ressources until the game mercilessly let me continue playing it half way through (A good fun 3 hours...) - of course at this point it was already over and chance of victory has long since escaped my grasp, but that's okay cause the game only takes half a day to complete anyway.

Another great example is that one of the factions starts with the throne power or whatever (It's thankfully been a while since I played this monstrosity), and if bidding for powers comes up in the very first round none has any real money and ties are broken by the throne power - harhar guess who now has the highest power 3 areas?? Those dudes! The fact that they can get a lead this early for no reason on than "it's just one of those draws, yo" just reeks of imbalances in this game. 

This game is all about staying friends with folks and it's all decided by having the most castles at the end. Well this usually just leads to the most "passive sit on your ass cause otherwise you are gonna get a mortal enemy which WILL cost you the game" gameplay (cause there is no other real reason to attack one dude over another) and then for the final round everyone just groups up their dudes and tries to capture as many castles as possible in the final round - hoping that the others decide to go a route that spares your castles - if they do - congrats you won. What an accomplishment after bloody half a day of sitting around having random event cards tell you how well you are doing. 

Hate hate hate hate! It's so bad! I haven't even scratched the surface of the many many flaws in this game!

I would rather play bloody Risk than this cause at least in Risk there is no illusion of it being good and no massive random imbalanced during the game. At least risk doesn't go "screw you, I'm taking all your stuff" right in the middel (or beginning or end) of the game.


The only good thing I can say is that I guess it's thematic and looks good, cause just like the TV show you get to watch all the things you fought for die for no reason.

RATING: 6
In the second edition of A Game of Thrones: The Board Game, three to six players take on the roles of the great Houses of the Seven Kingdoms of Westeros, as they vie for control of the Iron Throne through the use of diplomacy and warfare. Based on the best-selling A Song of Ice and Fire series of fantasy novels by George R.R. Martin, A Game of Thrones is an epic board game in which it will take more than military might to win. Will you take power through force, use honeyed words to coerce your way onto the throne, or rally the townsfolk to your side? Through strategic planning, masterful diplomacy, and clever card play, spread your influence over Westeros!

RATING: 1
I was looking forward to this just from the general praise I had heard from friends and from boardgamegeek. 

Unfortunately I had picked the Lannisters without really understanding their predicament of being the "worst" faction to start off with. Before the game even started, one of the players harped on for about 5 minutes as to why my faction was the worst.

What proceeded was nothing short of bemusement. A few turns in, everyone realised I was about to win before even I knew I was about to win. I had stumbled my way into the headlights of all the other players. 

The players then planned to attack me on multiple fronts which was expected. What was not expected was an attack from a player who I believed could not reach me. I had misread the board state due to some devious placement of ships on the opposite side of the board. I was not impressed.

Board games to me are a competition in decision making. If I'm stuck gaming with people who believe that you need to win at all costs then that's the day I quit this hobby for good. As to whether I'll play this game again? I doubt it. The map is busy, the components are clumsy (the armies defending territories are pretty big and obscure the board), the cards are numerous (so you need to learn all the cards before you can even successfully navigate the mire of combat) and the game play isn't that compelling. Why bother?

I realised from this experience that I really dislike heavy negotiation games. Watching players plead with each, make snide remarks or cajole weaker players into bad positions is not my idea of entertainment. It's like being around a whole bunch of marketers trying to sell you things that you're not interested in. That's pretty much my idea of hell.

RATING: 5.5
Don't get it wrong - it's a well designed game. But...

Personally, I really dislike all this Game of Thrones related stuff - but neither the TV series nor the book is what I'm writing here about. I heard that this is a good game, so I put away my antipathies and gave it a try. The first edition was released long before GoT started to be a buzz, thus it may not be just another GoT-themed product. And I wasn't underwhelmed.

A Game of Thrones is a very good board game. It's mechanics gives you the variety of "nice" feelings (really they're driven by the game mechanics!) - power struggle, backstabbing, treason and more. All time you or other players have to make a choice who will take some kind of negative effects ("take that"), you have to make alliances and break it a bit later, you have to be coldblooded - that's the only way to the Throne.

Game of Thrones fans should be pleased, but I'm not one of them, I definitely do not enjoy all of this. It's just not the way I like to spend time playing board games. That's all. It's not for me.

With all above said, the game has a few flaws. Sometimes it feels too random, even a careful strategy can't save you from a bad roll (or rather a bad draw), your army will be annihilated and you have to wait for another random event to rebuild it. Houses are unbalanced - some are undoubtedly better situated than others. The map is a bit vague - it's easy to miss a boundary and make a terrible mistake. Last but not least, it has a horribly long playing time (even 4-5h!) - without this I would consider playing it from time to time (when in a right mood).

RATING: 7
interesting and not too complex war game. Only the play time is a bit too long, it easily takes up to more than 3 hours to play.
Every player gets a house, a region and the same starting conditions. Then you start with secretely plan your moves and play the tokens hidden. You can move, cancel an opponents action, recruit, get victory points, etc.
Then everyone reveals and makes their actions.
I really enjoyed it, but the cunning and cutthroat theme or feeling does not come into play here. You will hardly make any deals with the other players which is a bit of a pity.
There is also the "kingmaker" problem. If someone does not really want to play anymore he is likely to give an opponent an easy victory.
But all in all this is a very cool game

RATING: 7
The game takes too long. I'll start with that. It takes too long to learn, and it takes too long to play a pilot game, and it takes too long with experienced players. But it is fun. It is like risk, minus the frustrating and often ridiculous luck dependency. 

The biggest issue other than the length of the game is the constant bickering about the balance. I don't think it is broken. Imbalanced is when a player can do much more than you, inherently. In this game, some players start in different locations, some making it harder to break out, but this is not a 2 player game. It is a diplomatic game. If you feel you are at a disadvantage, and you truly are, no one is going to pick on you, you inch your way and you take a different route to the same goal. If you consider that a major flaw in the game design, then this game is not for you. You may also want to rule out a whole bunch of games that do not have identical/near-identical starts.

RATING: 4
The combat is really interesting, and without dice there's a lot of clever tactics. On top of that, I really like that troops aren't always killed, so you can regroup and try again. Voting for powers is really cool too. However, mustering troops completely kills this game for me. It is so important to muster troops that being shut out leaves you out of the game, especially early on, when building your army is so important. Even worse is you have no idea when extra musters will appear from the deck, or when a voting round to get a spot on the raven track will appear. In my first game voting happened once on the first turn and didn't happen again until the 7th, leaving anyone that didn't get to muster shut out of the game. For a 3-5 hour game this is completely unacceptable. On top of that not knowing how long you're bidding for your slot inexcusable. Power is hard to come by, and considering how decisive and calculable combat is, it's ridiculous to have a randomly determined bid for the ability to muster troops which you may get for only one turn, or you may have for the rest of the game.

RATING: 9
An amazing area control game for 6 players. There is never any down time for players since turns are integrated and the mechanics are very well thought out. No dice means it's almost all strategy with just a little luck thrown in here and there with a few global event cards.

Only complaint, and it may go away with more plays and learning the strategy. The win condition seems just a little shallow. It makes story sense (No one army in Westeros tried to take the whole continent) and it makes time sense (getting 7 castles can take you 4 hours as is). I guess when I go to sit down at a conquest game I imagine more, well conquest.

Still a great game though! It's easy to pick up and learn and I think it will be my "long game" choice for a good long time.

RATING: 10
I love the first edition of this game, and this is basically the same thing.  There's a few teeny-tiny changes, but you're going to either love them both, or hate them both.  It feels a little bit cheap giving a 10 out to both versions, but they're so similar that there's no way to make a distinction in the ratings.

As for a direct comparison, I give the nod to the first edition.  The pieces are a bit better in the second edition, especially the player screens and the card art, but the first edition expansions allow the game to play well with 4-6 players, while the second only truly shines with exactly six.  The added options of the expansions are icing on the cake.

10th play was with four players (and expansion) and again quite nice 4 hours. A good game.

11th play (2nd with 6 players) in 5 hours, it was good. It could have been faster, if some players would have not been so tired or so much affected by drinking a day before... :) So the game is now faster than before and it feels good. Very close to updating the rating to 8.

12th play with three players and it took almost 6 hours now...

14th play was with 6 and only 5 hours. We played during the day, not on the evening, thou. :)

RATING: 8
I am in love with this game.If i could find 5 other like minded individuals i would play it every week.
The second edition board looks 'busier' than the first edition one,but almost every aspect of the production is well executed and beautiful.
Gameplay is elegant and simple,nothing too intricate or complicated ruleswise,although the strategy and diplomacy inherent in the game might make for heavy going in your first couple of games.Combat is card based,with almost perfect knowledge of your odds of winning,which i know some people will not like,but the meat and potatoes of the game comes from the constantly shifting alliances within the game.Every player needs the support of other players during the game,but not neccesarily the same players and not neccesarily for long.Players who go it alone invariably lose.
Nice to play a conquest game where the sides are not balanced,where negotiation is as important(if not moreso) than military might and where there is a finite turn limit.
 The only down side is that the game does NOT scale down well.5 or 6 players is the absolute minimum really,with 6 being the sweet spot.

RATING: 9

RATING: 6
This re-release does a nice job of folding various expansion mechanics from the first edition into the core rules of the second. I'm not a huge fan of the new playing pieces, as certain colors and shapes can be hard to tell apart, but for the most part the new edition is very nicely put together. I'm glad Fantasy Flight was able to re-release this design, because it would have been a tragedy if this game wasn't available to take advantage of the atmosphere that the HBO adaptation has created in Pop culture.

The game itself is neat experience that manages to deliver some of the backstabbing military moves and broken promises that are such an integral part of the books. Like most games that rely on social interaction to create intrigue and mistrust, A Game of Thrones will only work if the players let it. If they are not willing to engage in copious amounts of table talk or gang-up on players that leave themselves exposed then the game can become somewhat stale and predictable. This game is meant for the Diplomacy crowd, so if you aren't willing to ride the razor's edge of a table flip, then you had best stay away.

RATING: 7
I like this more than my memory of the first edition, though it's been several years since I played the older one. This was largely because I felt like there was less blind bidding this time out.

My biggest issue with it is that it seems the event decks have a bigger impact on the outcome of the game than they ought to for a game of this length. Having core mechanics like mustering or supply tied largely to chance seems hard to adjust for; in my first play of this, we finished the game without adjusting supply once, and it turned out all 3 supply cards were at the bottom of the deck. I appreciate some element of chance in games, but not when I feel like the outcome largely depended on when the cards came up.

Not to sound too down on it - after all, one still has to put themselves in a situation where they can take advantage of the cards should they arrive. Like a lot of Fantasy Flight's bigger titles that I've played, the game is very good at creating a specific mood (in this case, Diplomacy-like negotiations) while being perhaps overly complex mechanically and not necessarily satisfying on its own. And I still think it's not nearly as good as the similar Starcraft game.

RATING: 10
[i]Rating for the full 6-player experience (the base game just doesn't work properly with fewer players):[/i]

I'll be honest - I get to play this game a couple of times a year at best. But whan I finally do, oh what a treat it is. This is a fantastic game with super strong, immersive theme. Look no further if you want a board game that combines strategy with diplomacy. Playing this game for the first time was a breakthrough moment for me, I immediately wanted to explore the whole world of board games.

But as much as I love it, it's not without its flaws:
- out of the box this game really works with 6 players only, there are some serious balance issues when playing with fewer players. Fortunately A Feast for Crows expansion fixes problems connected with 4p games (and there's a nice fan-made printable 5p overlay available on BGG);
- the gameplay can really drag when playing with beginners or people prone to AP, be ready for a 4-6 hour session with the full player count (A Dance with Dragons expansion significantly cuts the overall length so get it if you want a shorter game with 6 players);
- static setup forces players to act in a certain way - early turns may feel a little scripted (this concerns primarily early conflict between Lannisters and Greyjoys). 

Still, whenever someone suggests playing GoT, I'll be the first guy at the table. A Game of Thrones is unlike anything else. One of the finest "dudes on the map" games out there.

[i]I own the following expansions: A Dance with Dragons, A Feast for Crows.[/i]

RATING: 7

RATING: 8.9
The game that got my board game group started. Thematically it's one of the best games I have. It really captures the essence of the A Song of Ice and Fire series of strategy, politics, alliances and betrayal. An well crafted game but after 30+ games it has a few flaws:
1. With less than 6 players it is a little imbalanced and I wouldn't really bother with 3 or 4 player games
2. It's a fairly long game (although with our experience we can bang out a 6 player game in less than 2 hours)
3. The raid order is "cheap" in that it reduces the amount of potential alliances in the game

RATING: 9
Loved it. Haven't yet played a 4-player game, but the 5 and 6 player variants were great, and even the 3-player was surprisingly good. 

I'd note that playing a good game seems to require a little bit of curating. In other words, games can be really imbalanced if an experienced player doesn't prompt people on a couple major strategy points before you start. Namely, I think it's important for everyone to realize immediately that because everyone starts in close proximity, alliances can be really important. I also think it's important to set a precedent for what those alliances can be. Game-long alliances can be really unfair. I prefer a turn-by-turn non-aggression pact kind of thing which offers a lot more balance and flexibility.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Really good asymetrical area majority game.  However, our first game was with 4 players, and it seems the board layout favors the player with the Baratheon house, where that player should be able to win the game fairly easily.  The reason is that you win the game by controlling castles, and most of the castles are on the Baratheon side of the board, where that player is free to just move in and take them without having to fight off the players on the other side of the board.  With 5 or 6 players, that advantage should be mostly gone.  Nonetheless, the game moves quickly (though it's not short), and there's plenty of meat for advanced gamers.

RATING: 6
I loved the first three books of A Song of Ice and Fire, but was sadly let down by book four, and all but the very end of book five. Therefore this game appeals to me greatly. I used to rarely, if ever, have the chance to play any six player game, especially if they were remotely long or heavy. This has changed, and a new very good friend even owned this game and brought it along to a scheduled event to introduce all of us to the game.

First of all this game does a great job of capturing its theme. I am definitely open to play again, but will perhaps not clamor for it, because it felt a bit long. Granted, we were all beginners, and I already learned a hell of a lot, and made mistakes I'm not likely to make again.

If it wasn't an event game this would see a hell of a lot of play, and if someone could convince me that a full six player game could take as little as 3-4 hours, then I'd gladly play this frequently.

I'm curious to play the two expansions. Both A Feast for Crows to check out the four player game with House Arryn, and especially the stacked A Dance with Dragons for a six player game up to date with the end of book five of A Song of Ice and Fire, and a much more urgent start position.

One disappointment to me was how limited the negotiation aspect felt. Sure, this could easily be chalked up as a rookie mistake or misunderstanding, but I played Tyrell, and there was just not much reason for me and House Stark to ever really negotiate over anything in my so far one and only game.

This game definitely deserves a second play to lock in my rating.

RATING: 4
I really dislike this game. Firstly I dislike the fact that the richest get way too much power in the game as they get access to these awesome game-changing items you can bid for, while the other guys sitting with few resources sit like ducks while the others make the game. It's fine to give an edge to the players who are doing good in the game, but with this one it feels so irreversible. There is NOTHING you can do to come back in the game. Secondly, it is sooooo slow to play. Lots of waiting time, planning phase is AWFUL and very little thrill in return. Even fights are uninteresting. Focuses too much on the combat aspect, I thought Game of Thrones was more about the intrigues between the houses rather than plain war even if I understand it also is an aspect of it. It felt like we played Risk and that the goodies were just for the guys sitting with piles of resources while I looked at the only castle I owned all game. Also, there seems to be huge balance issues with this game, so yeah this is a no-go for me. This said I am a huge fan of the TV series. I just didn't feel the theme in the game aside from the board and the Direwolf/Lion on the back of my cards. Rest is non-existent.

RATING: 8.75
A really intense game that will start off with everyone avoiding conflict and generally not much happening in the first three turns but then someone will do something closer to the second half and everything will really kick off, alliances are formed and wars started. This game will really make your heart race in the last 2 or three turns as you scramble to make sure that everything goes right so that you can come out on top. I would not recommend playing with less than five players. If you do play with less then what I do is not allow players in most of the Dornish lands as this ensures more conflict and stops the Tyrells and Baratheons from having some free castles. I also don't play with the tides of battle cards, I never have I liked the idea of having less luck aspects in the game. This is a great game to play if you can find some competitive friends.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Ok game that combines strategy, diplomacy and bluffing but also has some annoying random elements in it and with unbalanced factions due to geography and cardeffects. 

Good points : it has subtile strategies and layers that are not always visible to new players. Combat is done well and without dice. Secret action selection phases are very cool and interesting mechanic. 
 
Some minor points : the supply mechanic is sometimes a little bit too restrictive. Not everything feels very well balanced. It requires at least 5 players, while 6 is very good. Do not play it with 3 or 4. The event cards should be turned visible for the next turn, so players can plan a strategy for them. A little bit stale on negotiating side, not that much diplomacy eventually. Combat is slow since there are neutral area's to be captured first. 

On balance: The balance is bad in this game. I usually play house Lannister but they are the most difficult to play due to horrible starting position. Lannister player is very dependant on what Greyjoy does and it's very probable that they'll never win if all players know what they are doing. 
It's as if the designers didn't care about balance and just hoped that diplomacy would balance the game, but that's not very intelligent design. 

On popularity: now that everyone knows about agot I find it a bit odd that this is the official boardgame for Agot that people will get into their hands, as this is a pretty complex game for new gamers.

RATING: 8
Great game for 6 players, would recommend using the alternate rules for 4 players (north blocked off, only southern families used).  Take time to really learn the rules and research any you are unsure of.  There are quite a few that can be misinterpreted at first, but once played correctly can completely change the game.

RATING: 6
I like games that try to pick up where Diplomacy left off, but seems to have many balance issues and AP problems. At least it's shorter than Diplomacy. But it seems like Diplomacy plus a lot of work, which still ultimately comes down the diplomacy part.

Having the Supply event not happen often, and especially if it happens late the first time, can seriously affect the game.

RATING: 10
Лучшая стратегия всех времен и народов. Для тех, кто может оставить все обиды за столом. Союзы и предательство. Отвага и удары в спину... И классная механика! Раздача приказов, треки короны, меча и ворона, влияние, снабжение, несложный подсчет боя... Всё очень хорошо переплетается между собой.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Rating after 1 play; unlikely it'll be more. 
- High learning curve. 
- Game seems to be for 6 only (which we had)
- Wildling track which affects players based on bids, not proximity to the north, seems to be a hugely tacked on mechanic.
- Only 1 influence track REALLY matters. 
- Card play to determine battle outcome didn't appeal to me: tacked on and erratic results due to the texts. 
- Player elimination by strangulation: it's harder and harder to fight back when you're lagging meaning your decline rate will increase.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A savage war game that plays brilliantly.  One of the few that's actually better with more players - fill all the slots and prepare for a brutal blood bath of war, negoatiations, and betrayal.
